 <hr>
 <p align="CENTER"><font size="-1" color="GREEN"><i>The Treasury of David</i>, by Charles Haddon Spurgeon, [1869-85], at sacred-texts.com</font></p><hr> <h3 align="center"><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#001">Psalms Chapter 119</a></h3> 
 <p align="center"><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa001.htm#001">Psalms</a></p> 
 <p><span class="margnote"><font color="GREEN" size="-1"><a name="an_000">psa 119:0</a></font></span></p>
 <p>Title. - There is no title to this Psalm, neither is any author's name mentioned. It is The Longest Psalm, and this is a sufficiently distinctive name for it. It equals in bulk twenty-two Psalms of the average length of the Songs of Degrees. Nor is it long only; for it equally excels in breadth of thought, depth of meaning, and height of fervour. It is like the celestial city which lieth four-square, and the height and the breadth of it are equal. Many superficial readers have imagined that it harps upon one string, and abounds in pious repetitions and redundancies; but this arises from the shallowness of the reader's own mind: those who have studied this divine hymn, and carefully noted each line of it, are amazed at the variety and profundity of the thought. Using only a Jew words, tire writer has produced permutations and combinations of meaning which display his holy familiarity with his subject, and the sanctified ingenuity of his mind. He never repeats himself; for if the same sentiment recurs it is placed in a fresh connection, and so exhibits another interesting shade of meaning. The more one studies it the fresher it becomes. As those who drink the Nile water like it better every time they take a draught, so does this Psalm become the more full and fascinating the oftener you turn to it. It contains no idle word; the grapes of this cluster are almost to bursting lull with the new wine of the kingdom. The more you look into this mirror of a gracious heart the more you will see in it. Placid on the surface as the sea of glass before the eternal throne, it yet contains within its depths an ocean of fire, and those who devoutly gaze into it shall not only see the brightness, but feel the glow of the sacred flame. It is loaded with holy sense, and is as weighty as it is bulky. Again and again have we cried while studying it, "Oh the depths!" Yet these depths are hidden beneath an apparent simplicity, as Augustine has well and wisely said, and this makes the exposition all the more difficult. Its obscurity is hidden beneath a veil of light, and hence only those discover it who are in thorough earnest, not only to look on the word, but, like the angels, to look into it.
 </p><p>The Psalm is alphabetical. Eight stanzas commence with one letter, and then another eight with the next letter, and so the whole Psalm proceeds by octonaries quite through the twenty-two letters of the Hebrew alphabet. Besides which, there are multitudes of appositions of sense, and others of those structural formalities with which the oriental mind is pleased, - formalities very similar to those in which our older poets indulged. The Holy Spirit thus deigned to speak to men in farms which were attractive to the attention and helpful to the memory. He is often plain or elegant in his manner, but he does not disdain to be quaint or formal if thereby his design of instruction can be the more surely reached. He does not despise even contracted and artificial modes of speech, if by their use he can fix this teaching upon the mind. Isaac Taylor has worthily set forth the lesson of this fact: - "In the strictest sense this composition is conditioned; nevertheless in the highest sense is it an utterance of spiritual life; and in thus finding these seemingly opposed elements, intimately commingled as they are throughout this Psalm, a lesson full of meaning is silently conveyed to those who shall receive it - that the conveyance of the things of God to the human spirit is in no way damaged or impeded, much less is it deflected or vitiated by its subjugation to those modes of utterance which most of all bespeak their adaptation to the infancy and the childlike capacity of the recipient."
 </p><p>Author. - The fashion among modern writers is, as far as possible, to take every Psalm from David. As the critics of this school are usually unsound in doctrine and unspiritual in tone, we gravitate in the opposite direction, from a natural suspicion of everything which comes from so unsatisfactory a quarter. We believe that David wrote this Psalm. It is Davidic in tone and expression, and it tallies with David's experience in many interesting points. In our youth our teacher called it "David's pocket book," and we incline to the opinion then expressed that here we have the royal diary written at various times throughout a long life. No, we cannot give up this Psalm to the enemy. "This is David's spoil." After long reading an author one gets to know his style, and a measure of discernment is acquired by which his composition is detected even if his name be concealed; we feel a kind of critical certainty that the hand of David is in this thing, yea, that it is altogether his own.
 </p><p>Subject. - The one theme is the word of the Lord. The Psalmist sets his subject in many lights, and treats of it in divers ways, but he seldom omits to mention the word of the Lord in each verse under some one or other of the many names by which he knows it; and even if the name be not there, the subject is still heartily pursued in every stanza. He who wrote this wonderful song was saturated with those books of Scripture which he possessed. Andrew Bonar tells of a simple Christian in a farmhouse who had meditated the Bible through three times. This is precisely what this Psalmist had done, - he had gone past reading into meditation. Like Luther, David had shaken every fruit-tree in God's garden, and gathered golden fruit therefrom. "The most," says Martin Boos, "read their Bibles like cows that stand in the thick grass, and trample under their feet the finest flowers and herbs." It is to be feared that we too often do the like. This is a miserable way of treating the pages of inspiration. May the Lord prevent us from repeating that sin while reading this precious Psalm,
 </p><p>There is an evident growth in the subject matter. The earlier verses are of such a character as to lend themselves to the hypothesis that the author was a young man, while many of the later passages could only have suggested themselves to age and wisdom. In every portion, however, it is the fruit of deep experience, careful observation, and earnest meditation. If David did not write it, there must have lived another believer of exactly the same order of mind as David, and he must have addicted himself to psalmody with equal ardour, and have been an equally hearty lover of Holy Writ.
 </p><p>Our best improvement of this sacred composition will come through getting our minds into intense sympathy with its subject. In order to this, we might do well to commit it to memory. Philip Henry's daughter wrote in her diary, "I have of late taken some pains to learn by heart Psalm 119, and have made some progress therein." She was a sensible, godly woman. Having done this, we should consider the fulness, certainty, clearness, and sweetness of the word of God, since by such reflections we are likely to be stirred up to a warm affection for it. What favoured beings are those to whom the Eternal God has written a letter in his own hand and style. What ardour of devotion, what diligence of composition can produce a worthy eulogium for the divine testimonies! If ever one such has fallen from the pen of man it is this<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#001">Psa 119:1</a>, which might well be called the holy soul's soliloquy before an open Bible.
 </p><p>This sacred ode is a little Bible, the Scriptures condensed, a mass of Bibline, Holy Writ rewritten in holy emotions and actions. Blessed are they who can read and understand these saintly aphorisms; they shall find golden apples in this true Hesperides, and come to reckon that this Psalm, like the whole Scripture which it praises, is a pearl island, or, better still, a garden of sweet flowers. 
 </p><p>Hints to Preachers
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#001">Psa 119:1</a>. - "Blessed." True blessedness lies in - 
 </p><p>I. Defilement avoided by the word.
 </p><p>II. Delight experienced in the word.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#001">Psa 119:1</a>. - Spiritual pedestrians are often mentioned in this Psalm. Model travellers are described in this passage. Observe, - 
 </p><p>I. Their Character: "Undefiled." They are so
 </p><p>(1) in Christ: found in him; complete; accepted. They are so
 </p><p>(2) by Christ: His spirit, truth, and grace are in them. "Chosen generation," "peculiar people."
 </p><p>II. Their path: "the law of the Lord." This path is
 </p><p>(1) Conspicuous - high, visible, distinguished from every other.
 </p><p>(2) Ancient. The old path. Holiness is older than sin, wisdom than folly, life than death, joy than sorrow.
 </p><p>(3) Safe. Christ has repaired it. Apart from his work none can pass safely over. He has brought down mountains, raised up valleys, made crooked places straight, and rough places smooth. He has driven away the lion.
 </p><p>(4) Narrow. It has a fence of commands on one side, and of prohibitions on the other. It is entered by a strait gate, which renders it necessary for the great to become as little children.
 </p><p>III. Their progress: "walk." Not only talk, but step in the footprints of Jesus. Follow the law-fulfiller. They proceed in the exercise of his graces, in the exhibition of his virtues, in the fulfilment of his injunctions, and in the enjoyment of his favours.
 </p><p>IV. Their happiness: "Blessed." They have unfailing help, suitable company, animating prospects on the way. - W. Jackson, 1882.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#001">Psa 119:1</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#002">Psa 119:2</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#003">Psa 119:3</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Positive and Negative Beatitudes of Being.
 </p><p>II. Six Con - Lions of Peace with God.
 </p><p>1. Purity.
 </p><p>2. Obedience.
 </p><p>3. Fidelity.
 </p><p>4. Seeking.
 </p><p>5. Integrity.
 </p><p>6. Following. - William Durban, 1882.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#002">Psa 119:2</a>. - "Blessed are they that keep his testimonies, and that seek him with the whole heart."
 </p><p>I. The sacred Quest: "Seek him." He has been sought among the trees, the hills, the planets, the stars. He has been sought in his own defaced image, man. He has been sought amid the mysterious wheels of Providence. But these quests have often been prompted simply by intellect, or compelled by conscience, and have therefore resulted but in a cold, faint light. He has been sought in the word which this Psalm so highly extols, when it has led up the smoke-covered and gleaming peaks of Sinai. It has been followed, when it has led beneath the olives of Gethsemane to witness a mysterious struggle in blood-sweating and anguish; to Calvary, where, in the place of a skull, life and immortality are brought to light. The sacred quest but there begins.
 </p><p>II. The Conduct of the Quest. Seekers might be mistakenly dejected by so literal an interpretation of the "whole heart." We do not hesitate to say a stream is in its whole volume flowing toward sea while there are little side creeks in which the water eddies backward; or to say the tide is coming despite receding waves; or that spring is upon us despite haft-storm and biting wind. Indication of,
 </p><p>1. Unity.
 </p><p>2. Intensity.
 </p><p>3. Determination.
 </p><p>No one conducts this quest aright who is not prompted to or sustained in it by the gracious Spirit.
 </p><p>III. Blessedness both in the pursuit and issue.
 </p><p>1. Blessedness in the bitterness of penitence. The door-handle touched by him drops of myrrh. The rising sun sends kindling beams upon the highest peaks.
 </p><p>2. Blessedness in the gladsome findings of salvation and adoption.
 </p><p>3. Blessedness in the perpetual pursuit. - William Anderson, 1882.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#002">Psa 119:2</a>. - The double blessing.
 </p><p>I. On keeping the testimonies.
 </p><p>II. On seeking the Lord.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#002">Psa 119:2</a>. - "That seek him with the whole heart."
 </p><p>I. Seek what? God himself. No peace until he is found.
 </p><p>II. Seek where? In his testimonies.
 </p><p>1. By studying them.
 </p><p>2. By keeping to them.
 </p><p>III. Seek how? With the whole heart. - George Rogers.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#002">Psa 119:2</a>. - Seeking for God.
 </p><p>I. The Psalmist's way of seeking God.
 </p><p>1. He sought God with the heart. Only the heart can find God. Sight fails. "The scientific method" fails. All reason fails. Only love and trust can succeed. Love sees much where all other perception finds nothing. Faith generally goes with discovery, and nowhere so much as in finding God.
 </p><p>2. He sought God with all his heart.
 </p><p>(1) Half-heartedness seldom finds anything worth having.
 </p><p>(2) Half-heartedness shows contempt for God.
 </p><p>(3) God will not reveal himself to half-heartedness. It would be putting the highest premium possible upon indifference.
 </p><p>II. The Psalmist's plea in seeking God: "Let me not wander from thy commandments."
 </p><p>1. God's commandments lead, presently into his own presence. If we take even the moral law, every one of the ten commandments leads away from the world, and sin, into that seclusion of holiness in which he hides. It is thus with all the commandments of the Scriptures.
 </p><p>2. The earnestness of the soul's search for God becomes, in itself, a plea with God that he will be found of us. God, who loves importunity in prayer, loves it no less when it takes the form of searching with all the heart. He who seeks with all the heart finds special encouragement to pray: "Let me not wander from thy commandments." - F. G. Marchant.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#002">Psa 119:2</a>. - "That seek him." We must remember six conditions required in them who would seek the Lord rightly.
 </p><p>I. We must seek him in Christ the Mediator. <a href="../../../bib/kjv/joh014.htm#006">Joh 14:6</a>.
 </p><p>II. We must seek him in truth. <a href="../../../bib/kjv/jer010.htm#010">Jer 10:10</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/joh004.htm#024">Joh 4:24</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa007.htm#006">Psa 7:6</a>.
 </p><p>III. We must seek him in holiness. <a href="../../../bib/kjv/ti2002.htm#019">Ti2 2:19</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/heb012.htm#014">Heb 12:14</a>; 1 John 3.
 </p><p>IV. We must seek him above all things and for himself.
 </p><p>V. We must seek him by the light of his own word.
 </p><p>VI. We must seek him diligently and with perseverance, never resting till we find him, with the spouse in the Canticles. - William Cowper.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#002">Psa 119:2</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#004">Psa 119:4</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#005">Psa 119:5</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#008">Psa 119:8</a>. - "Blessed are they that keep." "Thou hast commanded us to keep." "O that my ways were directed to keep." "I will keep." The blessedness of keeping God's precepts - displayed (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#002">Psa 119:2</a>), commanded (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#004">Psa 119:4</a>), prayed for (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#005">Psa 119:5</a>), resolved upon (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#008">Psa 119:8</a>). - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#003">Psa 119:3</a>. - "They also do no iniquity." They work no iniquity with - 
 </p><p>1. Purpose of heart;
 </p><p>2. Delight;
 </p><p>3. Perseverance;
 </p><p>4. Nor at all when the heart is fully sanctified unto God; Christ dwelling in it by faith, and casting out sin. - Adam Clarke.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#003">Psa 119:3</a>. - The relation between negative and positive virtue. Or walking with God the best preventive of iniquity.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#004">Psa 119:4</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Take notice of the law-giver, "Thou." Not thy equal, or one that will be baffled, but the great God.
 </p><p>II. He hath interposed his authority: "hast commanded."
 </p><p>III. The nature of this obedience, or the thing commanded: "To keep thy precepts." - T. Manton.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#004">Psa 119:4</a>. - The supplementary commandment. God having ordained the moral law, supplements it with a commandment prescribing the manner of keeping it. Hence:
 </p><p>I. God is not indifferent to men's treatment of his law - whether they observe, neglect, or defy it.
 </p><p>II. When observed, God discriminates the spirit of its observance, whether slavish, partial, careless, or diligent.
 </p><p>III. There is but one spirit of obedience, whether slavish, partial, careless, or diligent.
 </p><p>IV. There is but one spirit of obedience which satisfies God's requirements. "Diligently" implies an obedience which is, - careful to ascertain the law - prompt to fulfil it (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#060">Psa 119:60</a>) - unreserved - love-inspired ("diligently," old meaning, through the Latin, "lovingly," <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#047">Psa 119:47</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#097">Psa 119:97</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#113">Psa 119:113</a>).
 </p><p>V. Does our obedience come up to this standard? - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#004">Psa 119:4</a>. - Not only is service commanded, but the manner of it. Heartiness, care, perseverance required, because without these it will not be true, uniform, or victorious over difficulty.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#004">Psa 119:4</a>. - How to obey: "Diligently."
 </p><p>1. Not partially, but fully.
 </p><p>2. Not doubtfully, but confidently.
 </p><p>3. Not reluctantly, but readily.
 </p><p>4. Not slovenly, but carefully.
 </p><p>5. Not coldly, but earnestly.
 </p><p>6. Not fitfully, but regularly. - W. J.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#004">Psa 119:4</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#005">Psa 119:5</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#006">Psa 119:6</a>. - A willing recognition (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#004">Psa 119:4</a>). An ardent aspiration (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#005">Psa 119:5</a>). A happy consequence (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#006">Psa 119:6</a>). - W. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#005">Psa 119:5</a>. - The prayer of the gracious.
 </p><p>I. Suggested by each preceding clause of blessing.
 </p><p>II. By a consciousness of failure.
 </p><p>III. By a loving clinging to the Lord.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#005">Psa 119:5</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The end desired: "To keep thy statutes." Not to be safe merely, or happy, but holy.
 </p><p>II. The help implored.
 </p><p>1. To understand the divine precepts.
 </p><p>2. To keep them. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#005">Psa 119:5</a>. - Longing to obey.
 </p><p>1. It is a noble aspiration. There is nothing grander than the desire to do this except the doing of it.
 </p><p>2. It is a spiritual aspiration. Not the offspring of our carnal nature. It is the heart of God in the new creature.
 </p><p>3. It is a practicable aspiration. We sometimes sigh for the impossible. But this may be attained by divine grace.
 </p><p>4. It is an intense aspiration. It is the "Oh!" of a burning wish.
 </p><p>5. It is an influential aspiration. It does not evaporate in sighs. It is a mighty incentive implanted by grace which will not let us rest without holiness. - W. J.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#006">Psa 119:6</a>. - See "Spurgeon's Sermons," No. 1443: "A Clear Conscience."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#006">Psa 119:6</a>. - Holy confidence the offspring of universal obedience.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#006">Psa 119:6</a>. - The armour of proof.
 </p><p>I. Universal obedience will give unabashed confidence - 
 </p><p>1. Before the criticising world.
 </p><p>2. In the court of conscience.
 </p><p>3. At the throne of grace.
 </p><p>4. In the day of judgment.
 </p><p>II. But our obedience is far from universal, and leaves us open to - 
 </p><p>1. The world's shafts.
 </p><p>2. The rebukes of conscience.
 </p><p>3. It paralyses our prayers, and,
 </p><p>4. It dares not appear for us at the bar of God.
 </p><p>III. Then let us by faith wrap ourselves in the perfect righteousness of Christ. Our answer to the world's cavil. We are not faultless, and for salvation we rest wholly on another. This righteousness is - 
 </p><p>1. The salve of our wounded conscience.
 </p><p>2. Our mighty plea in prayer.
 </p><p>3. Our triumphant vindication in the judgment day. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#006">Psa 119:6</a>. - Topic: - Self-respect depends on respect for one greater than self. - W. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#007">Psa 119:7</a>. - The best of praise, the best of learning, the best of blendings, viz., praise and holiness.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#007">Psa 119:7</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The professor of sacred music, "I will praise."
 </p><p>II. The subject of his song - "Thee."
 </p><p>III. The instrument, "Heart."
 </p><p>IV. The instrument tuned: "Uprightness of heart."
 </p><p>V. The musician's training academy: "Judgments." - W. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#007">Psa 119:7</a>. - Learning and praising.
 </p><p>I. They are two spiritual exercises. It is possible for learners and singers to be carnal and sensual; but in this case they are employed about the righteous ends, works, and ways of the Lord.
 </p><p>II. They are two appropriate exercises. What can be more seemly than to learn of God and to praise him?
 </p><p>III. They are two profitable exercises. The expectations of the most utilitarian are surpassed. The pleasure and the profit yield abundant reward. Heart, head, life are all benefited.
 </p><p>IV. They are two mutually-assisting exercises. In the one we are receptive, and in the other communicative. By the one we are fitted to do the other. By the former we are stimulated to do the latter. How wonderfully the lesson is turned into a song, and the learner into a singer. - W. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#007">Psa 119:7</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Deficiency confessed, "When I shall have learned." This is essential to growth. It is an admission all can truly make.
 </p><p>II. Progress anticipated. He gave his heart to the work of learning. He sought divine help.
 </p><p>III. Praise promised. He promised it to God alone. He vowed it should be sincere: "with upright heart." - W. Williams, 1882.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#008">Psa 119:8</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. A hopeful resolve for life.
 </p><p>II. A dreadful fear.
 </p><p>III. A series of considerations removing the fear.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#008">Psa 119:8</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The resolution: "I will keep," etc.
 </p><p>II. The petition: "O forsake me not utterly."
 </p><p>1. Filial submission. I deserve it occasionally.
 </p><p>2. Filial confidence. "Not utterly."
 </p><p>III. The connection between the two. Obedience without prayer and prayer without obedience are equally in vain. To make headway both oars must be applied. God cannot abide lazy beggars, who while they can get anything by asking will not work. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#008">Psa 119:8</a>. - "O forsake me not utterly." Divine desertion deprecated.
 </p><p>I. The anguished prayer.
 </p><p>1. Sovereign forsakings. Sovereignty is not arbitrariness or capriciousness; perhaps its right definition is mysterious kingly love; unknown now, but justified when revealed.
 </p><p>2. Vicarious forsakings.
 </p><p>3. Forsakings on account of sin. David, Jonah, and Peter. The seven churches of Asia; the Jews. But to know what "utter" both in regard to degree and time means, we must go to hell. Like one trembling on the very verge of hell, he prays. Like belated traveller, in vast wood and surrounded by beasts of prey, sighs at day's departure. Like the watch on the raft, seeing the sail that he has shouted himself hoarse to stop fading away in the sky line.
 </p><p>II. Its doctrinal foundation. Where he condescendeth to dwell, his abode is perpetual. He can only utterly forsake us because he was deceived in us. He can only utterly forsake because baffled. Both imply blasphemy. Thou who hatest putting away, thou who hast never yet utterly forsaken any saint, make not me the solitary exception.
 </p><p>III. Historical certainty of answer. The saint and the church in all time delivered. It may tarry till "eventide," as in Cowper's case. His face bore after death an expression of delighted surprise. - W. A.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#009">Psa 119:9</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The young man's question.
 </p><p>II. The wise man's reply.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#009">Psa 119:9</a>. - In the word of God, when applied to the heart by the Spirit of God, there is,
 </p><p>I. A sufficiency of light to discover to men the need of cleansing their way.
 </p><p>II. Sufficiency of energy for the cleansing their way.
 </p><p>III. A sufficiency of pleasure to encourage them to choose to cleanse their way.
 </p><p>IV. A sufficiency of support to sustain them in their cleansed way. - Theophilus Jones, in a "Sermon to the Young," 1829.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#009">Psa 119:9</a>. - The word of God provides for the cleansing of the way,
 </p><p>I. By pointing out to the young man the evil of the way.
 </p><p>II. By discovering an infallible remedy for the disorders of his nature - the salvation that is by Jesus Christ.
 </p><p>III. By becoming a directory in all the paths of duty to which he may be called. - Daniel Wilson, 1828.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#009">Psa 119:9</a>. - The Psalmist's rules for the attainment of holiness deduced from his own experience.
 </p><p>1. Seek God with thy "whole heart" (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#002">Psa 119:2</a>). Be truly sensible of your wants.
 </p><p>2. Keep and remember what God says (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#011">Psa 119:11</a>): "Thy word have I hidden," etc.
 </p><p>3. Reduce all this to practice (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#011">Psa 119:11</a>): "That I might not sin against thee."
 </p><p>4. Bless God for what he has given (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#012">Psa 119:12</a>): "Blessed art thou," etc.
 </p><p>5. Ask more (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#012">Psa 119:12</a>): "Teach me thy statutes."
 </p><p>6. Be ready to communicate his knowledge to others (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#013">Psa 119:13</a>): "With my lips have I declared."
 </p><p>7. Let it have a due effect on thy own heart (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#014">Psa 119:14</a>), "I have rejoiced," etc.
 </p><p>8. Meditate frequently upon them (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#015">Psa 119:15</a>): "I will meditate," etc.
 </p><p>9. Deeply reflect on them (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#016">Psa 119:16</a>): "I will have respect," etc. As food undigested will not nourish the body, so the word of God not considered with deep meditation and reflection will not feed the soul.
 </p><p>10. Having pursued the above course he should continue in it, and then his happiness would be secured (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#016">Psa 119:16</a>): "I will not forget thy word: I will (in consequence) delight myself in thy statutes." - Adam Clarke.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#009">Psa 119:9</a>. - A question and answer for the young. The Bible is a book for young people. Here it intimates,
 </p><p>I. That the young man's way needs to be cleansed. His way of thinking, feeling, speaking, acting.
 </p><p>II. That he must take an active part in the work. The efficient cause in the operation is God. Other good influences are also at work. But the young man must be in hearty and practical sympathy with the work.
 </p><p>III. That he must use the Bible for the purpose. This records facts, presents incitations, enjoins precepts, utters promises, and sets up examples, all which are adapted to make a young man holy. By reading, studying, and imitating the Scriptures in a lowly and prayerful spirit the young shall escape pollution and ornament society. - W. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#009">Psa 119:9</a>. - A word to the young.
 </p><p>I. Show how the young man is in special danger of defiling his way. Through,
 </p><p>1. His strong passions.
 </p><p>2. His immature judgment.
 </p><p>3. His inexperience.
 </p><p>4. His rash self-sufficiency.
 </p><p>5. His light companions, and,
 </p><p>6. His general heedlessness.
 </p><p>II. The circumspection he should use to cleanse his way. "Taking heed,"
 </p><p>1. Of his evil propensities.
 </p><p>2. Of his companions.
 </p><p>3. Of his pursuits.
 </p><p>4. Of the tendencies of all he does.
 </p><p>III. The infallible guide by which his circumspection is to be regulated, "according to thy word" - that is to say,
 </p><p>1. Its precepts.
 </p><p>2. Its examples.
 </p><p>3. Its motives.
 </p><p>4. Its warnings.
 </p><p>5. Its allurements. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#010">Psa 119:10</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. A grateful review.
 </p><p>II. An anxious forecast.
 </p><p>III. A commendable prayer.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#010">Psa 119:10</a>. - The believer's two great solicitudes.
 </p><p>1. What he is anxious to find: "I have sought thee."
 </p><p>2. What he is afraid of losing: "Thy commandments." - W. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#010">Psa 119:10</a>. - Sincerity not self-sufficiency.
 </p><p>I. The believer must be conscious of whole-heartedness in seeking God.
 </p><p>II. But consciousness of sincerity does not warrant self-sufficiency.
 </p><p>III. The most whole-hearted seeker must still look to divine grace to keep him from wandering. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#011">Psa 119:11</a>. - The best thing, in the best place, for the best of purposes.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#012">Psa 119:12</a>. - The blessedness of God, and the mode of entering into it.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#012">Psa 119:12</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. David gives glory to God: "Blessed art thou, O Lord."
 </p><p>II. He asks grace from God. - Matthew Henry.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#012">Psa 119:12</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. What it is, or how God doth teach us.
 </p><p>1. God doth teach us outwardly; by his ordinances, by the ministry of men.
 </p><p>2. Inwardly; by the inspiration and work of the Holy Ghost.
 </p><p>II. The necessity of his teaching.
 </p><p>III. The benefit and utility of it. - T. Manton.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#012">Psa 119:12</a>. - Desire for Divine Teaching excited by the Recognition of Divine Blessedness.
 </p><p>I. Unveil in some inadequate degree the happiness of the ever blessed God, arising from his purity, benevolence, love.
 </p><p>II. Show the way in which man may become partaker of that blessedness by conformity to his precepts.
 </p><p>III. Utter the prayer of the text. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#013">Psa 119:13</a>. - Speech fitly employed. It is occupied with a choice subject, a full subject, a subject profitable to men, and glorifying to God.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#014">Psa 119:14</a>. - Practical religion, the source of a comfort surpassing riches. It gives a man ease of mind, independence of carriage, weight of influence, and other matters supposed to arise out of wealth.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#014">Psa 119:14</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The subject of rejoicing. Not the "testimonies" merely, but their observances, "the way of," etc.
 </p><p>II. The rejoicing in that subject.
 </p><p>1. In its inward peace.
 </p><p>2. In its external consequences.
 </p><p>III. The degree of the rejoicing, "as much as," etc. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#014">Psa 119:14</a>. - The two scales of the balance. Whatever riches are good for, God's testimonies are good for.
 </p><p>I. Riches are desirable as the means of procuring the necessaries of life; but God's testimonies supply the necessities of the soul.
 </p><p>II. Riches are desirable as a means of procuring personal enjoyment; but God's testimonies produce the highest joy.
 </p><p>III. Riches are desirable as a means of attaining personal improvement; but God's testimonies are the highest educators.
 </p><p>IV. Riches are desirable as a means of doing good; but God's testimonies work the highest good. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#015">Psa 119:15</a>. - The contemplative and active life; their common food, object, and reward.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#016">Psa 119:16</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. What there is to be delighted in.
 </p><p>II. What comes of such delight "I will never forget."
 </p><p>III. What comes of such memory - more delight.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#017">Psa 119:17</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. A bountiful master.
 </p><p>II. A needy servant - begging for very life.
 </p><p>III. A suitable recompense: "and keep thy word."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#017">Psa 119:17</a>. - We are here taught,
 </p><p>I. That we owe our lives to God's mercy.
 </p><p>II. That therefore we ought to spend our lives in God's service. - Matthew Henry.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#018">Psa 119:18</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The precious casket: "thy law."
 </p><p>II. The invisible treasure: "wondrous things."
 </p><p>III. The miraculous eyesight, "that I may behold."
 </p><p>IV. The divine oculist, "Open thou mine eyes."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#018">Psa 119:18</a>. - The hidden wonders of the gospel. There are many hidden things in nature; many in our fellow men; so there are many in the Bible. The things of the Bible are hidden because of the blindness of man.
 </p><p>I. The blind man's sorrow: "Open mine eyes." I cannot see. I have eyes and see not. The pain of this conscious blindness when a man really feels it.
 </p><p>II. The blind man's conviction, "That I may behold wondrous," etc. There are wondrous things there to be seen. I am sure of it. There is a wonderful view, - 
 </p><p>(1) of sin;
 </p><p>(2) of hell, as its desert;
 </p><p>(3) of One ready to save;
 </p><p>(4) of perfect pardon;
 </p><p>(5) of God's love;
 </p><p>(6) of all-sufficient grace;
 </p><p>(7) of heaven.
 </p><p>III. The blind man's wisdom. The fault is in my eyes, not in thy word. "Open my eyes," and all will be well. The reason for not seeing is because the eyes are blinded by sin. There is nothing wanting in the Bible.
 </p><p>IV. The blind man's prayer: "Open thou mine eyes."
 </p><p>1. I cannot open them.
 </p><p>2. My dearest friends cannot.
 </p><p>3. Only thou canst. "Lord, I pray thee, now open them." Many seek to stop such praying. Be like Bartimeus who "cried so much the more."
 </p><p>V. The blind man's anticipation: "That I may behold."
 </p><p>1. The joy of a cured blind man when he is about to behold, for the first time, the beauties of nature.
 </p><p>2. The joy of the spiritually healed when they begin "looking unto Jesus."
 </p><p>3. The personal character of the joy: "Open thou mine eyes, that I may behold." I have hitherto had to see through the eyes of others. I would depend on other eyes no longer. The glad anticipation of Job: "Whom I shall see for myself, and mine eyes shall behold, and not another." - Frederick G. Merchant, 1882.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#018">Psa 119:18</a>. - God's word suited to man's sense of wonder.
 </p><p>I. We shall make some remarks on the sense of wonder in man, and what generally excites it. One of the first causes of wonder is the new or unexpected. The second source is to be found in things beautiful and grand. A third source is the mysterious which surrounds man - there are things unknowable.
 </p><p>II. God has made provision for this sense of wonder in his revealed word. The Bible addresses our sense of wonder by constantly presenting the new and unexpected to us; it sets before us things beautiful and grand. If we come to the third source of wonder, that which raises it to awe, it is the peculiar province of the Bible to deal with this.
 </p><p>III. The means we are to use in order to have God's word thus unfolded - the prayer of the Psalmist may be our guide - "Open thou mine eyes, that I may behold wondrous things out of thy law." - John Ker, of Glasgow, 1877.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#018">Psa 119:18</a>. - Wondrous sights for opened eyes.
 </p><p>I. The wondrous things in God's law. A wondrous rule of life. A wondrous curse against transgression. A wondrous redemption from the curse shadowed forth in the ceremonial law.
 </p><p>II. Special eyesight needed to behold them. They are spiritual things. Men are spiritually blind. <a href="../../../bib/kjv/co1002.htm#014">Co1 2:14</a>.
 </p><p>III. Personal prayer to the Great Opener of eyes. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#019">Psa 119:19</a>. - An insight into the divine will, the best assistance in our journey through the earth. Or, what I am; where I am; where I am going; how am I to get there?
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#019">Psa 119:19</a> (first clause). - The stranger in the earth.
 </p><p>I. A short exposition. The text means, - 
 </p><p>1. That the saint is not born of the earth.
 </p><p>2. That the saint is not known on earth.
 </p><p>3. The saint's portion is not upon the earth.
 </p><p>4. The saint is compassed with sorrows and trials upon earth.
 </p><p>5. The saint is soon to leave the earth.
 </p><p>II. A short application.
 </p><p>1. Do not be like the world.
 </p><p>2. Be prepared to be a sufferer on the earth.
 </p><p>3. Sit loose to the world.
 </p><p>4. Correspond with home.
 </p><p>5. Cherish brotherly love for your fellow-strangers on the earth.
 </p><p>6. Hasten home.
 </p><p>7. Press others to come with you. - Duncan Macgregor's Sermon in "The Shepherd in Israel," 1869.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#019">Psa 119:19</a>. - The stranger's prayer.
 </p><p>I. How he came to be a stranger in the earth. He was born again. He learned the manners of his foreign home. He spoke the language of his Fatherland; and so was misunderstood and rejected on earth.
 </p><p>II. How he longed after everything homelike. Home rules: "thy commandments." Home teaching, "hide not." Specially his Father's voice.
 </p><p>III. How in his loneliness he, solaced himself by communication with his Father.
 </p><p>IV. Would you not like to be a stranger? - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#020">Psa 119:20</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The word sought, and sought at all times.
 </p><p>II. The word sought, and sought with intense desire.
 </p><p>III. The word sought, and sought the more intensely the more it is found. It was because he had found so much in the word of the Lord already, that the soul of the Psalmist was breaking to find more. Those who have been once admitted to "the secret of the Lord" find their highest joy in knowing that secret still more fully. It is to those who know that secret that the promise is given - "He will shew them his covenant'" <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa025.htm#014">Psa 25:14</a>. - F. G. M.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#020">Psa 119:20</a>. - One of the best tests of character and prophecies of what a man will be, are his longings.
 </p><p>I. The saint's absorbing object, "Thy judgments." The word here is synonymous with the "word" of God.
 </p><p>1. The Psalmist greatly reverenced the word.
 </p><p>2. He intensely desired to know its contents.
 </p><p>3. He wishes to feed upon God's word.
 </p><p>4. He longed to obey it.
 </p><p>5. He longed to feel the power of God's judgments in his own heart.
 </p><p>II. The saint's ardent longings.
 </p><p>1. They constitute a living experience.
 </p><p>2. The expression used in the text represents a humble sense of imperfection.
 </p><p>3. It indicates an advanced experience.
 </p><p>4. It is an experience which we may term a bitter sweet.
 </p><p>5. These longings may become very wearying to a man's soul.
 </p><p>III. Cheering reflections.
 </p><p>1. God is at work in your soul.
 </p><p>2. The result of God's work is very precious.
 </p><p>3. It is leading on to something more precious.
 </p><p>4. The desire itself is doing you good.
 </p><p>5. It makes Christ precious. See "Spurgeon's Sermons," No. 1586, "Holy Longings."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#021">Psa 119:21</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The character of the proud.
 </p><p>II. God's dealings with them.
 </p><p>III. Our own relation to them.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#021">Psa 119:21</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The sin; "Err from the commandments."
 </p><p>1. By neglect; or,
 </p><p>2. By abuse of them.
 </p><p>II. Its origin - pride: pride of reason, of heart, of life.
 </p><p>III. Its punishment.
 </p><p>1. Rebuke.
 </p><p>2. Condemnation. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#023">Psa 119:23</a>. - Meditation.
 </p><p>I. Our best employment while others slander.
 </p><p>II. Our best comfort under their falsehood.
 </p><p>III. Our best preservative from a spirit of revenge.
 </p><p>IV. Our best mode of showing our superiority to their attacks.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#024">Psa 119:24</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. He reverenced them as God's testimonies.
 </p><p>II. He revelled in them as his delight.
 </p><p>III. He referred to them as his counsellors.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#025">Psa 119:25</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Nature and its tendency.
 </p><p>II. Grace and its mode of operation.
 </p><p>III. Both truths in their personal application.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#025">Psa 119:25</a>. - "Quicken thou me," etc.
 </p><p>I. There are many reasons why we should seek quickening.
 </p><p>1. Because of the deadening influence of the world. "My soul cleaveth," etc.
 </p><p>2. The influence of vanity (see <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#037">Psa 119:37</a>).
 </p><p>3. Because we are surrounded by deceivers (see <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#087">Psa 119:87, 19 119:88</a>).
 </p><p>4. Because of the effect of seasons of affliction upon us (see <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#007">Psa 119:7</a>).
 </p><p>II. Some of the motives for seeking quickening.
 </p><p>1. Because of what you are - a Christian; life seeks more life.
 </p><p>2. Because of what you ought to be.
 </p><p>3. Because of what we shall be.
 </p><p>4. In order to obedience (see <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#088">Psa 119:88</a>).
 </p><p>5. For your comfort (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#107">Psa 119:107</a> and <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#050">Psa 119:50</a>).
 </p><p>6. As the best security against the attacks of enemies (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#087">Psa 119:87-88</a>).
 </p><p>7. To invigorate our memories (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#093">Psa 119:93</a>).
 </p><p>8. Consider (as a motive to seek this quickening) the terrible consequences of losing spiritual life or, in other words, lacking it in its manifest display.
 </p><p>III. Some of the ways in which the quickening may be brought to us.
 </p><p>1. It must be by the Lord himself. "Quicken me, O Lord."
 </p><p>2. By the turning of the eyes (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#037">Psa 119:37</a>).
 </p><p>3. By the word (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#050">Psa 119:50</a>).
 </p><p>4. By the precepts (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#093">Psa 119:93</a>).
 </p><p>5. By affliction (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#107">Psa 119:107</a>).
 </p><p>6. By divine comforts.
 </p><p>IV. Enquire where are our pleas when we come before God to ask for quickening.
 </p><p>1. Our necessity (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#107">Psa 119:107</a>, etc.).
 </p><p>2. Our earnest desire (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#040">Psa 119:40</a>).
 </p><p>3. Appeal to God's righteousness (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#040">Psa 119:40</a>).
 </p><p>4. To his lovingkindness (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#088">Psa 119:88</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#149">Psa 119:149</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#156">Psa 119:156</a>).
 </p><p>5. The plea in the text: "according to thy word" (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#028">Psa 119:28</a> and <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#107">Psa 119:107</a>). See "Spurgeon's Sermons," No. 1350: "Enlivening and Invigorating."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#026">Psa 119:26</a>. - Confession. Absolution. Instruction.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#026">Psa 119:26</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The duty: "I have declared my ways" - made known my experience of thy word to others.
 </p><p>II. Its notice by God, "Thou heardest me."
 </p><p>III. Its reward. More knowledge will be given: "Teach me," etc. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#027">Psa 119:27</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. A student's prayer.
 </p><p>1. It deals with the main subject of the conversation which is to be that student's occupation - '"the way of God's precepts."
 </p><p>2. A confession is implied, "Make me," etc.
 </p><p>3. A great boon is asked to understand, to know, thy statutes.
 </p><p>4. The Fountain of all wisdom is applied to.
 </p><p>II. The occupation of the instructed man.
 </p><p>1. He testifies of God's works - his wondrous works - Christ's work for us; the Holy Spirit's work in us. The wonderful character of these works of God, a wide field for devout study.
 </p><p>2. He speaks very plainly: "I will talk," etc.
 </p><p>3. He will speak very frequently: "I will talk."
 </p><p>4. He will speak to the point: "So" - i.e., according to understanding.
 </p><p>III. The intimate relation between the prayer of the student and the pursuit that he subsequently followed. See "Spurgeon's Sermons," No. 1344: "The Student's Prayer."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#027">Psa 119:27</a>. - Education for the ministry.
 </p><p>I. The student at college: "Make me to understand." His lesson. His instructor. His application.
 </p><p>II. The preacher at his work: "So shall I talk," etc. His qualification. His theme. His manner. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#028">Psa 119:28</a>. - Heaviness, its cause, curse, and cure.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#029">Psa 119:29</a>. - The way of lying.
 </p><p>I. Describe the way of lying. Various paths, e.g., erroneous views of doctrine: false grounds of faith: looseness of practice: shrinking from the daily cross.
 </p><p>II. Show why it is thus named. It does not furnish its promised pleasures. It does not lead to its professed goal. It lies through the territory of the father of lies.
 </p><p>III. Notice the peculiarity in the prayer against it. Not remove me from, but remove from me: for the way of lying is within us.
 </p><p>IV. Our deliverance from the way of lying lies with God. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#029">Psa 119:29</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#030">Psa 119:30</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The way of lying, our wish to have it removed, and the method of answer.
 </p><p>II. The way of truth, our choice, and the method of carrying it out.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#031">Psa 119:31</a>. - Reasons for sticking to the Divine testimonies.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#031">Psa 119:31</a>. - A wholesome mixture.
 </p><p>I. Sturdy fidelity.
 </p><p>II. Self-distrust, and,
 </p><p>III. Importunate prayer. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#032">Psa 119:32</a>. - The Fettered Racer set free.
 </p><p>I. The course that invited him.
 </p><p>II. The shackles that bound him.
 </p><p>III. The impatience that prompted him.
 </p><p>IV. The Lord that freed him.
 </p><p>V. Now let him go. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#032">Psa 119:32</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Liberty desired.
 </p><p>II. Liberty rightly used. Or, the effect of the heart upon the feet.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#032">Psa 119:32</a>. - The text will give us occasion to speak,
 </p><p>I. Of the benefit of an enlarged heart.
 </p><p>II. The necessary precedency of this work on God's part, before there can be any serious bent or motion of heart towards God on our part.
 </p><p>III. The subsequent resolution of the saints to engage their hearts to live to God.
 </p><p>IV. With what earnestness, alacrity and vigour of spirit this work is to be carried on: "I will run." - T. Manton.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#032">Psa 119:32</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The way of obedience. "Thy commandments."
 </p><p>II. The duty of obedience, "I will run" - not stand still - not loiter - not creep - not walk, but run.
 </p><p>III. The life of obedience.
 </p><p>1. Where it lies - in the heart.
 </p><p>2. Whence it comes: "When thou shalt," etc.
 </p><p>3. What it does - enlarges the heart. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#033">Psa 119:33</a>. - In this prayer for grace observe,
 </p><p>1. The person to whom he prays. "O Lord."
 </p><p>2. The person for whom: "teach me."
 </p><p>3. The grace for which he prayeth, to be taught.
 </p><p>4. The object of this teaching: "The way of thy statutes." The teaching which he beggeth, is not speculative, but practical, to learn how to walk in the way of God. - T. Manton.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#033">Psa 119:33</a>. - The superior efficacy of divine teaching - it secures holy practice and insures its perpetuity.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#033">Psa 119:33</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#034">Psa 119:34</a>. - Light from above.
 </p><p>I. The blinding power of sin. "Teach me," i.e., "point out to me." "Give me understanding." Whatever may have been the original amount of light which came from eating from the tree of knowledge of good and evil, that light has long been insufficient.
 </p><p>1. Men need light to discern the right way from the wrong.
 </p><p>2. Men need light to understand the beauties of the right way. Such beauties line the way of truth on either hand, but only the God - taught mind appreciates them. Even Jesus, who is the way, the truth, and the life, is as a root out of a dry ground, till the mind is taught of the Lord. Sin is the cause of this blindness. The farther any man walks in the way of sin, the less can he see of the beauties of holiness.
 </p><p>II. The enlightening grace of the Lord. "Teach me." "Give me understanding." This grace,
 </p><p>1. May be boldly asked: "If any man lack wisdom let him ask of God."
 </p><p>2. Will be freely given. "Who giveth to all men liberally." "Ask, and it shall be given."
 </p><p>3. Will be amply sufficient. "I shall keep it unto the end." "I shall keep Thy law." To see is to follow.
 </p><p>III. The stimulating power of clearly revealed truth. "I shall observe it with my whole heart." To see is not only to follow, but to follow with love and gladness. It is written of the light which will come before the throne, "We shall be like him, for we shall see him as he is." "O thou, that dwellest between the Cherubim, shine forth," even here, on the way that leads to thy presence. - F. G. M.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#033">Psa 119:33-35</a>. - Alpha and Omega.
 </p><p>I. God, the giver of spiritual instruction: <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#033">Psa 119:33</a>.
 </p><p>II. Of spiritual understanding, without which this instruction is in vain: <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#034">Psa 119:34</a>.
 </p><p>III. Of grace for practical obedience when thus instructed: <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#035">Psa 119:35</a>.
 </p><p>IV. For whole-hearted obedience: <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#034">Psa 119:34</a>.
 </p><p>V. For final perseverance: <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#033">Psa 119:33</a>. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#033">Psa 119:33-36</a>. - Human Dependence on Divine help.
 </p><p>I. There can be no steady keeping in the way of the Lord without the Lord's guidance: <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#033">Psa 119:33</a>.
 </p><p>II. There can be no observing of the way with the heart without Divine light for the mind: <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#034">Psa 119:34</a>.
 </p><p>III. There can be no diligent pursuit of the way till divine energy be given to the will: <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#035">Psa 119:35</a>.
 </p><p>IV. There can be no true love of the way unless the heart be constrained by the love of God: <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#036">Psa 119:36</a>. He who said, "Without me ye can do nothing," is necessary for us to see the way, to understand the way, to walk in the way, and to love the way. - F. G. M.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#034">Psa 119:34</a>. - The influence of the understanding upon the heart, and the united power of understanding and heart over the life.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#034">Psa 119:34</a>. - Seeing and loving.
 </p><p>I. When men see they love (the whole verse).
 </p><p>II. When men love they see. Only the loving heart would have seen enough to write such a verse. - F. G. M.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#035">Psa 119:35</a>. - The prayer of a child, and the delight of a child. Or, Our pleasure in holiness a plea for grace.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#035">Psa 119:35</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Delight avowed.
 </p><p>II. Disinclination implied.
 </p><p>III. Constraint implored. - W. W.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#036">Psa 119:36</a>. - Holiness a cure for covetousness.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#036">Psa 119:36</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#112">Psa 119:112</a>. - The Co-operation of the Divine and the Human in Salvation.
 </p><p>I. It is God that worketh in you: <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#036">Psa 119:36</a>.
 </p><p>II. Therefore work out your own salvation with fear and trembling, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#112">Psa 119:112</a>. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#037">Psa 119:37</a>. - "Quicken thou me in thy way." This brief prayer - 
 </p><p>I. Deals with the believer's frequent need.
 </p><p>II. It directs us to the sole worker of quickening: "Thou."
 </p><p>III. It describes the sphere of renewed vigour: "in thy way."
 </p><p>IV. It denotes that there may be special reasons and special seasons for this prayer - times of temptation: <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#037">Psa 119:37</a>; seasons of affliction, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#107">Psa 119:107</a>; when called to some extraordinary service. See "Spurgeon's Sermons," No. 1073: "A Honeycomb."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#037">Psa 119:37</a>. - Here is,
 </p><p>I. Conversion from - '"vanity."
 </p><p>II. Conversion to - '"thy way.
 </p><p>III. Conversion by - '"Quicken thou me." - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#037">Psa 119:37</a>. - David prays,
 </p><p>(1) for restraining grace that he might be prevented and kept back from that which would hinder him in the way of his duty: "Turn away mine eyes from beholding vanity."
 </p><p>(2) He prays for constraining grace, that he might not only be kept from everything that would obstruct his progress heaven-ward, but that he might have that grace which was necessary to forward him in that progress: "Quicken thou me in thy way." - M. Henry.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#038">Psa 119:38</a>. - Confirmation. What? "Thy word established." To whom? "Unto thy servant." Why? "Who is devoted," etc.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#038">Psa 119:38</a>. - Fear of God evidences itself,
 </p><p>1. By a dread of his displeasure.
 </p><p>2. Desire of his favour.
 </p><p>3. Regard for his excellencies.
 </p><p>4. Submission to his will.
 </p><p>5. Gratitude for his benefits.
 </p><p>6. Conscientious obedience to his commands. - Charles Buck.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#038">Psa 119:38</a>. - The four kinds of fear.
 </p><p>1. The fear of man, by which we are led rather to do wrong than to suffer evil.
 </p><p>2. Servile fear, through which we are induced to avoid sin only from the dread of hell.
 </p><p>3. Initial fear, in which we avoid sin partly from the fear of hell, but partly also from the love of God, which is the fear of ordinary Christians.
 </p><p>4. Filial fear, when we are afraid to disobey God only and altogether from the love we bear him. <a href="../../../bib/kjv/jer032.htm#040">Jer 32:40</a>. - Ayguan, in J. Edward Vaux's "Preacher's Storehouse," 1878.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#039">Psa 119:39</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Man's judgment dreaded.
 </p><p>II. God's judgment approved.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#039">Psa 119:39</a>. - The reproach of inconsistency.
 </p><p>I. The dishonour caused by it (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/sa2012.htm#014">Sa2 12:14</a>).
 </p><p>II. The danger of incurring it.
 </p><p>III. The prayer against it. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#040">Psa 119:40</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Gracious longings experienced.
 </p><p>II. Great necessity felt - more life needed.
 </p><p>III. Wise petition offered.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#041">Psa 119:41</a>. - See "Spurgeon's Sermons," No. 1524, "Your Personal Salvation."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#041">Psa 119:41</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. God's mercies come to us unsought continually. His sparing mercies, temporal mercies, etc.
 </p><p>II. The chief outcome of God's mercies is his salvation. It is our greatest need; it is his greatest gift.
 </p><p>III. We should have a personal interest in this salvation - "Let thy mercies come also unto me."
 </p><p>IV. When we seek God's salvation, we may plead his promise. "according to thy word." - Horatio Wilkins, 1882.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#041">Psa 119:41</a>. - "Even me."
 </p><p>I. In me there is need of mercy.
 </p><p>II. To me mercy can come.
 </p><p>III. Thy salvation suits me.
 </p><p>IV. Special difficulties would daunt me.
 </p><p>V. Thy word encourages me.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#041">Psa 119:41</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Salvation is all of mercy.
 </p><p>II. All mercies are in salvation.
 </p><p>III. All men should be anxious for salvation to come to them.
 </p><p>IV. It can only come according to God's word. - W. W.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#041">Psa 119:41-43</a>. - A Comprehensive Prayer.
 </p><p>I. The possession of salvation, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#041">Psa 119:41</a>.
 </p><p>II. Is the power for defence, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#042">Psa 119:42</a>.
 </p><p>III. And the qualification for usefulness, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#043">Psa 119:43</a>. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#042">Psa 119:42</a>. - Faith's answer to reproach found in the fact that she trusts God's word.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#042">Psa 119:42</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#043">Psa 119:43</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#047">Psa 119:47</a>. - Faith, hope, and love. "I trust." "I have hoped." "I have loved." Faith warring, hope testifying, love obeying.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#043">Psa 119:43</a>. - How the true preacher could be silenced, and his plea that he may not be so.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#044">Psa 119:44</a>. - The perpetuity of gracious living. On what it is conditioned. "So." How entirely it is consistent with free agency. "I keep." How continuous it is, and how eternal.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#044">Psa 119:44</a>. - Heaven begun below.
 </p><p>I. The present life of the believer - keeping God's law.
 </p><p>II. The continual care of the believer - to keep God's law.
 </p><p>III. The eternal prospect of the believer - keeping God's law for ever and ever. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#045">Psa 119:45-47</a>. - Liberty of walk. Liberty of speech. Liberty of heart.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#045">Psa 119:45-48</a>. - The true freeman enjoys - 
 </p><p>1. Free walk with God.
 </p><p>2. Free talk about God.
 </p><p>3. Free love unto God.
 </p><p>4. Free exercise of soul,
 </p><p>(1) in holy practice;
 </p><p>(2) in heavenly meditation. - W. Durban.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#045">Psa 119:45-48</a>. - Five things the Psalmist promiseth himself here in the strength of God's grace.
 </p><p>1. That he should be free and easy in his duty, "I will walk at liberty."
 </p><p>2. That he should be bold and courageous in his duty, "I will speak of thy testimonies also before kings."
 </p><p>3. That he should be cheerful and pleasant in his duty. "I will delight myself in thy commandments."
 </p><p>4. That he should be diligent and vigorous in his duty: "I will delight myself in thy commandments."
 </p><p>5. That he should be thoughtful and considerate in his duty: "I will meditate in thy statutes." M. Henry.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#046">Psa 119:46-48</a>. - Lips, heart, and hands.
 </p><p>I. Public profession of God's word ("I will speak," <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#046">Psa 119:46</a>) must be warranted by - 
 </p><p>II. Private delight in God's word ("I will delight, myself," <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#047">Psa 119:47</a>), which must result in - 
 </p><p>III. Practical obedience to God's word ("I will lift up my hands," <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#048">Psa 119:48</a>).
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#046">Psa 119:46</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The truly earnest must speak.
 </p><p>II. They are at no loss for good subjects - "Thy testimonies." The range is boundless - the variety endless.
 </p><p>III. They never fear any audience. "before kings." - W. W.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#048">Psa 119:48</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Love renewing its activity.
 </p><p>II. Love refreshing itself with spiritual food.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#048">Psa 119:48</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Scripture in the hand for reading. Often in the hand.
 </p><p>II. In the mind for meditation: "I will meditate," etc.
 </p><p>III. In the heart for love: "Which I have loved." - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#048">Psa 119:48</a>. - Religion engaged the whole manhood of David: hands, heart, head.
 </p><p>I. The uplifted hands.
 </p><p>1. Taking an oath of allegiance to God's word. <a href="../../../bib/kjv/gen014.htm#022">Gen 14:22</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/eze020.htm#028">Eze 20:28</a>. To receive its doctrines, obey its precepts, regard its warnings, uphold its honour.
 </p><p>2. Imploring a blessing upon God's word. <a href="../../../bib/kjv/gen048.htm#014">Gen 48:14</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/lev009.htm#022">Lev 9:22</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/luk024.htm#050">Luk 24:50</a>. That its light might spread: "Fly abroad, thou mighty gospel;" that its influence may become universal.
 </p><p>II. The loyal heart.
 </p><p>1. This accounts for uplifted hands. He had loved the word himself. Religion is inward first, then outward. We must love it before we are anxious to spread it.
 </p><p>2. But what accounts for the loyal heart? The word had brought him salvation, yielded him sustenance, afforded him guidance. We love the word for its joyous effects upon ourselves.
 </p><p>III. The studious mind.
 </p><p>1. Devout meditation the best employment.
 </p><p>2. The word of God affords a grand field for it.
 </p><p>3. To meditate in it learn to love it: "have loved," "will meditate." - W. W.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#048">Psa 119:48</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. God's commandments loved. We love the law when we love the Lawgiver. We love his will only when our hearts are reconciled and renewed. Hence the need of spiritual renewal.
 </p><p>II. God's commandments the subject of prayer: "My hands also will I lift up." Perowne says, "The expression denotes the act of prayer." We may pray for a fuller knowledge, a deeper experience, a readier and more perfect obedience.
 </p><p>III. A theme for meditation. Amidst the hurry of outward activities we must not forget the need of quiet meditation. -H. W.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#049">Psa 119:49</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The personality of the word: "the word unto thy servant."
 </p><p>II. The application of the word: "upon which thou hast caused me to hope."
 </p><p>III. The pleading of the word: "Remember the word," etc.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#049">Psa 119:49</a>. - The word of hope.
 </p><p>I. God's word the foundation of human hope. (The fact of a revelation. The substance of the revelation.)
 </p><p>II. Particular words of God which have been found peculiarly hope-enkindling.
 </p><p>III. The pleading of such words at the throne of grace. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#050">Psa 119:50</a>. - Each man has his own affliction and his own consolation. Quickened piety the best comfort. The word the means of it.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#050">Psa 119:50</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The need of consolation.
 </p><p>II. The consolation needed. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#051">Psa 119:51</a>. - The proud man's contumely, and the gracious man's constancy.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#051">Psa 119:51</a>. - Fidelity in the face of contempt.
 </p><p>I. The proud deride the believer's subjection to God's law.
 </p><p>II. They ridicule the believer's delight in God's service.
 </p><p>III. They are met by the believer's resolution to cleave to God. <a href="../../../bib/kjv/sa2006.htm#020">Sa2 6:20</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/sa2006.htm#022">Sa2 6:22</a>. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#052">Psa 119:52</a>. - Comfort derived from a review of the ancient doings of the Lord towards the wicked and his people.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#052">Psa 119:52</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The dead speaking to the living.
 </p><p>II. The living listening to the dead. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#052">Psa 119:52</a>. - Sweet water from a dark well.
 </p><p>I. God's judgments are calculated to inspire terror.
 </p><p>II. But they prove God's superintending care over the world.
 </p><p>III. They are ever against sin, and for holiness.
 </p><p>IV. In all times of judgment God delivers his people. Noah, Lot, etc.
 </p><p>V. Therefore God's judgments are a source of comfort to the believer. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#053">Psa 119:53</a>. - The sensations of godly men at the sight of sinners' horror at their crime, their perseverance in it, their rejection of grace, and their end.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#053">Psa 119:53</a>. - Horror-stricken.
 </p><p>I. The guilt and danger of impenitent sinners.
 </p><p>II. The horror and concern of godly spectators.
 </p><p>III. The prayer and labour which such concern should dictate. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#054">Psa 119:54</a>. - Here is - 
 </p><p>I. Light in darkness.
 </p><p>II. Companionship in solitude.
 </p><p>III. Activity in rest: "house of pilgrimage." - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#054">Psa 119:54</a>. - The cheerful pilgrim.
 </p><p>I. A good man views his residence in this world as only the house of his pilgrimage.
 </p><p>II. The situation, however disadvantageous, admits of cheerfulness.
 </p><p>III. The sources of his joy are derived from the Scriptures. - W. Jay.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#054">Psa 119:54</a>. - See "Spurgeon's Sermons," No. 1652: "The Singing Pilgrim."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#055">Psa 119:55</a> with <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#049">Psa 119:49</a>. - "Remember." "I have remembered."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#055">Psa 119:55</a>. - Night memories. Day duties. How they act and react upon each other.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#055">Psa 119:55</a>. - Dark nights. Bright memories. Right results. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#055">Psa 119:55</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Happy though restless night.
 </p><p>II. Happy though busy day. - W. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#056">Psa 119:56</a>. - The gains of godliness; or, what a man gets through holy living.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#056">Psa 119:56</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The duty: "I kept thy precepts."
 </p><p>II. Its reward: "This I had," etc. Protection: "this I had." Guidance: "this I had." Prosperity: "this I had." Consolation: "this I had." - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#057">Psa 119:57</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The infinite possession: "Thou art my portion, O Lord." Notice - 
 </p><p>1. A clear distinction made by the Psalmist between his portion and that of the ungodly here and hereafter: See Ps. 73.
 </p><p>2. A positive claim: "Thou art my portion, O Lord." This "portion" is boundless, abiding, appropriate, satisfying, elevating, all of grace.
 </p><p>II. The appropriate resolution: "I have said that I would keep thy words."
 </p><p>1. Notice the preface: "I have said."
 </p><p>2. The link between the portion possessed and the resolution made.
 </p><p>3. The work of keeping God's words. Keep him who is the Word - Christ Jesus. Keep the word of the gospel - doctrines, precepts, promises (kept in the heart to comfort the believer). This blessed subject suggests a solemn contrast. See the portion of that servant who did not keep his Lord's word: Matt. 29:48-51. See "Spurgeon's Sermons," No. 1372: "God our Portion, and his Word our Treasure."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#057">Psa 119:57</a> (first clause). - The believer's portion.
 </p><p>I. Show the validity of his claim: "my."
 </p><p>1. A gift by covenant, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/heb008.htm#010">Heb 8:10-13</a>.
 </p><p>2. Involved in joint heirship with Christ: <a href="../../../bib/kjv/rom008.htm#017">Rom 8:17</a>.
 </p><p>3. Confirmed by the experience of faith.
 </p><p>II. Survey the superlative value of his possession: "The Lord."
 </p><p>1. Absolutely good.
 </p><p>2. Infinitely precious.
 </p><p>3. Inexhaustibly full.
 </p><p>4. Everlastingly sure.
 </p><p>III. Suggest a method of deriving the greatest present advantage from it.
 </p><p>1. Meditate much upon God, under the conviction that he is your portion.
 </p><p>2. Carry all cares to him, and cast every burden on him.
 </p><p>3. Refer every temptation to the word of his law, and every doubt to the word of his promise.
 </p><p>4. Draw largely upon his riches to meet every need as it arises. - John Field, 1882.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#057">Psa 119:57</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#058">Psa 119:58</a>. - The believer's estate, profession, and petition.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#058">Psa 119:58</a>. - The soul's sunshine.
 </p><p>I. God's favour the one thing needful.
 </p><p>II. Whole-heartedness the one mode of entreating it.
 </p><p>III. Covenant mercy the one plea for obtaining it. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#058">Psa 119:58</a>. - We may learn how a seeker may come to enjoy saving favour, by a careful study of - 
 </p><p>I. The Profession: "I intreated thy favour with my whole heart."
 </p><p>1. What he did: "I intreated." Heb. "I painfully sought thy face." Earnest desire. Importunate supplication. Painful sorrow for sin.
 </p><p>2. How he did it: "With my whole heart." The intellect, affections, will, all engaged and concentrating effort. Otherwise, seeking is solemn trifling. This only worthy of our purpose, pleasing to God, and successful.
 </p><p>3. The evidence that we are doing it. Frequent prayer, searching the word, often enquiring. The first and main business - Giving up for Christ.
 </p><p>II. The Petition: "Be merciful unto me."
 </p><p>1. God's favour to be expected on the terms of mercy only.
 </p><p>2. Happily, this is a prayer every sinner can and should use.
 </p><p>3. Blessedly true it is, that it never fails.
 </p><p>III. The Plea: "According to thy word."
 </p><p>1. A plea that cannot be gainsaid is a great thing in an entreaty.
 </p><p>2. The promise of God is just such a plea.
 </p><p>3. Seek it out, lay hold of it, and urge it. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#059">Psa 119:59</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Self-examination: "I thought on" my private "ways" - my social ways - my sacred ways - my public ways.
 </p><p>II. Its advantages: "And turned my feet," etc. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#059">Psa 119:59</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Unthinking and straying.
 </p><p>II. Thinking and turning. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#059">Psa 119:59</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Conviction.
 </p><p>II. Conversion. - W. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#059">Psa 119:59</a>. - Thinking on our own ways. Enquire,
 </p><p>I. Why so generally neglected?
 </p><p>1. Want of courage.
 </p><p>2. Occupied too much.
 </p><p>3. Unpleasant, and therefore the chief care of many is to banish it.
 </p><p>II. When is it wisely conducted?
 </p><p>1. When honestly engaged in.
 </p><p>2. When thoroughly carried out.
 </p><p>3. When Scripture is made the referee and standard.
 </p><p>4. When Divine help is sought.
 </p><p>III. What end will it serve?
 </p><p>1. Turn us from our own ways with shame and penitence.
 </p><p>2. Turn us to God's testimonies with earnestness, reverence, and hopefulness. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#059">Psa 119:59</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Right thinking: "I thought on my ways."
 </p><p>1. That this thought upon his ways caused the Psalmist dissatisfaction is evident.
 </p><p>2. Right thinking upon our ways will suggest a practical change.
 </p><p>3. The retrospect we take of our life should suggest that any turn we make should be Godward: "Unto thy testimonies."
 </p><p>4. Right thinking also suggests that such a turning is possible.
 </p><p>II. Right turning. The turn was - 
 </p><p>1. Complete.
 </p><p>2. Practical.
 </p><p>3. Spiritual.
 </p><p>4. Immediate.
 </p><p>5. It must be a divine work. See "Spurgeon's Sermons," No. 1181, "Thinking and Turning."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#060">Psa 119:60</a>. - The dangers of delay. The reasons for prompt action.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#060">Psa 119:60</a>. - A sermon to loiterers.
 </p><p>I. Reflection. Keeping God's commandments is my duty; is my welfare. Commandments delayed may be never kept. Delay is in itself disobedience. Alacrity is the soul of obedience.
 </p><p>II. Resolve. I will make haste and delay not. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#060">Psa 119:60</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Quick.
 </p><p>II. Sure. - W. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#060">Psa 119:60</a>. - Procrastination considered in its most important application; that is, to religion.
 </p><p>I. This procrastination is irrational.
 </p><p>II. It is unpleasant, disagreeable, painful.
 </p><p>III. It is disgraceful.
 </p><p>IV. It is sinful, and that is the highest degree.
 </p><p>V. It is dangerous. - John Angell James.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#061">Psa 119:61</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Spiritual highway-robbery.
 </p><p>II. The traveller keeping his road. Or, what enemies can do, and what they cannot do.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#062">Psa 119:62</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The duty of gratitude, "give thanks."
 </p><p>II. The subject for gratitude, "thy righteous judgments."
 </p><p>III. The season for gratitude, at night as well as in the day. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#062">Psa 119:62</a>. - Up in the night. Singing in the night. Reasons for such singular conduct.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#062">Psa 119:62</a>. - The nightingale.
 </p><p>I. A natural association of thought, "midnight" and "judgments." Exod. 12, etc.
 </p><p>II. An incongruous association of feeling, "thanks" and "judgments"
 </p><p>III. A full justification of this apparent incongruity: "thanks because of thy righteous judgments."
 </p><p>IV. A vigorous performance of at, incumbent duty, "at midnight I will rise to give thanks." - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#063">Psa 119:63</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. True religion is friendly.
 </p><p>II. Our friendliness should be catholic.
 </p><p>III. Our friendliness should be discriminating.
 </p><p>IV. Such friendliness is most useful.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#063">Psa 119:63</a>. - Of good and bad company. How to avoid the one, and improve the other. See W. Bridge's Sermon, in his works, vol. v., p. 90. Tegg's edition, 1845.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#063">Psa 119:63</a>. - The believer's choice of companions.
 </p><p>I. Ought to be decided by their piety, "Them that fear thee."
 </p><p>II. Is directed by their conduct, "Them that keep thy precepts."
 </p><p>III. Should be extended as far as possible, "All."
 </p><p>IV. Involves reciprocal obligation, "I am a companion." - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#064">Psa 119:64</a>. - The sum and substance of this verse will be comprised in these five propositions: - 
 </p><p>I. That saving knowledge is a benefit that must be asked of God.
 </p><p>II. That this benefit cannot be too often or sufficiently enough asked, it is his continual request.
 </p><p>III. In asking, we are encouraged by the bounty or mercy of God.
 </p><p>IV. That God is merciful all his creatures declare.
 </p><p>V. That his goodness to all his creatures should confirm us in hoping for saving grace or spiritual good things - T. Manton.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#064">Psa 119:64</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Observations in the school of nature.
 </p><p>II. Supplications to enter the school of grace.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#064">Psa 119:64</a>. - The mercy of God in nature and his mercy as revealed in the word.
 </p><p>I. The one excellent; the other super-excellent.
 </p><p>II. The one easily given; the other coming through a great sacrifice.
 </p><p>III. The one may be enjoyed, and even increase condemnation; the other, if enjoyed, is sure salvation.
 </p><p>IV. The one should lead to repentance; the other is specially adapted for the penitent's restoration to holiness. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#065">Psa 119:65</a>. - The servant giving his master a character; or, experience tallying with Scripture, two fruitful themes.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#065">Psa 119:65</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Experience confirmed by the word.
 </p><p>II. The word confirmed by experience. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#065">Psa 119:65</a>. - A servant's story.
 </p><p>I. Although he knew my faults he engaged me.
