 </p><p>II. Although I am so far beneath him, yet, he familiarly teaches me.
 </p><p>III. Although I am always ailing, he is very kind to me in my afflictions.
 </p><p>IV. Although I am one of the meanest of his servants, he permits me to feast at his own table.
 </p><p>V. Although I do little work, he will pay me good wages.
 </p><p>VI. Although I am to have such great wages, I have very many perquisites.
 </p><p>VII. Although my Master is all this to me (can you believe it?) I murmur and repine at him if he crosses me in anything. Application: - 
 </p><p>1. Does not the word "servant" sound like a misnomer? - '"not servants but I have called you friends."
 </p><p>2. Though he calls me "friend," I shall never cease to call him "Master." - Richard Andrew Griffin, in "Stems and Twigs."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#066">Psa 119:66</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Singular faith: "I have believed thy commandment."
 </p><p>II. Special petition based upon it: "Teach me."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#066">Psa 119:66</a>. - The value of a good judgment to sound knowledge.
 </p><p>I. It carefully discriminates between truth and error.
 </p><p>II. It puts each truth in its proper relation to other truths.
 </p><p>III. It holds every truth firmly, but has the greater care for the more important.
 </p><p>IV. It rather avoids the curious and the speculative, but really loves the plain and useful.
 </p><p>V. Knowing that truths are rightly held only, when applied, it turns all to practical account.
 </p><p>VI. Knowing also, that good food may, under some circumstances, become poisonous, it is careful in its selection and use of truths. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#067">Psa 119:67</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The dangers of prosperity.
 </p><p>II. The benefits of adversity. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#067">Psa 119:67</a>. - The restraining power of affliction.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#067">Psa 119:67</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#071">Psa 119:71</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#075">Psa 119:75</a>. - Affliction thrice viewed and thrice blessed.
 </p><p>I. Before affliction: straying.
 </p><p>II. In affliction: learning.
 </p><p>III. After affliction: knowing. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#068">Psa 119:68</a>. - The double plea for a choice blessing. The goodness of God the hope of our ignorance.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#068">Psa 119:68</a>. - "Thou art good and doest good." The nature and work of God are manifest in nature, providence, grace, and glory. They are morally good; beneficially good; perfectly good; immeasurably good; immutably good; experimentally good; satisfactorily good. - W. J.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#068">Psa 119:68</a> (first clause). - A sermon on God's goodness.
 </p><p>I. The perfectness of it.
 </p><p>II. The proofs of it.
 </p><p>III. The power it should have over us. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#069">Psa 119:69</a>. - Whole-hearted obedience the best solace under slander; the best answer to it; and the best way of converting the slanderers.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#070">Psa 119:70</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Fatty degeneration of the heart.
 </p><p>II. Thorough regeneration of the heart.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#070">Psa 119:70</a>. - A fatty heart.
 </p><p>I. The diagnosis of the disease.
 </p><p>II. Its symptoms. Pride; no delight in God, nor in his law; dislike to his people; readiness to lie: <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#069">Psa 119:69</a>.
 </p><p>III. Its fatal character.
 </p><p>IV. Its only cure. <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa051.htm#010">Psa 51:10</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/eze036.htm#026">Eze 36:26</a>. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#071">Psa 119:71</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. David knew what was good for him.
 </p><p>II. David learned what is good essentially. Active obedience is learned by passive obedience.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#071">Psa 119:71</a>. - Affliction an instructor.
 </p><p>I. Never welcomed: "Have been."
 </p><p>II. Often impatiently endured.
 </p><p>III. Always gratefully remembered: "It is good," etc.
 </p><p>IV. Efficient for a perverse scholar: "That I might learn."
 </p><p>V. Indispensable in the education of all. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#071">Psa 119:71</a>. - The school of affliction.
 </p><p>I. The reluctant scholar sent to school.
 </p><p>II. The scholar's hard lesson.
 </p><p>III. The scholar's blessed learning.
 </p><p>IV. The scholar's sweet reflection. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#072">Psa 119:72</a>. - The advantages of riches far excelled by the blessings of the word.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#072">Psa 119:72</a>. - A valuation.
 </p><p>I. The saints' high estimate of God's law.
 </p><p>II. Show when it was formed: in affliction: <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#071">Psa 119:71</a>.
 </p><p>III. Vindicate its truth - by illustrating the hollowness of riches, and the satisfaction found in godliness. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#072">Psa 119:72</a>. - The word, better than gold and silver.
 </p><p>I. It gives what gold and silver cannot purchase.
 </p><p>II. Without what it gives, gold and silver may be a curse.
 </p><p>III. Without gold and silver, it may yield its treasure more freely and fully than with them.
 </p><p>IV. The word and what it gives shall rejoice the heart when gold and silver shall be useless to their disappointed worshippers. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#072">Psa 119:72</a>. - "The law of the mouth is better," etc.
 </p><p>I. It is more refining, and makes me a better man.
 </p><p>II. It is more enriching, and makes me a wealthier man.
 </p><p>III. It is more distinguishing, and makes me a greater man.
 </p><p>IV. It is more sustaining, and makes me a stronger man.
 </p><p>V. It is more preserving, and makes me a safer man.
 </p><p>VI. It is more satisfying and makes me a happier man.
 </p><p>VII. It is more lasting, and better suited to me as an immortal man. - W. J.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#073">Psa 119:73</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Consider the Lord's great care in our creation.
 </p><p>II. See in it a reason for his perfecting the new creation within us.
 </p><p>III. Observe the method of this perfecting.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#074">Psa 119:74</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The encouraging influence of good men upon others.
 </p><p>II. The Instructive influence of others upon them. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#074">Psa 119:74</a>. - Converse with a tried but steadfast believer is a source of gladness to the children of God.
 </p><p>I. He has a thrilling tale of experience to tell.
 </p><p>II. He has valuable counsels and cautions to give.
 </p><p>III. He is a monument of God's faithfulness, confirming the hope of others.
 </p><p>IV. He is an epistle of Christ, written expressly to illustrate the preciousness and the power of the gospel. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#075">Psa 119:75</a>. - Experimental knowledge, positive, personal, glorifying to God, consoling to the saints.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#076">Psa 119:76</a>. - Comfort.
 </p><p>I. May be a matter of prayer.
 </p><p>II. Is provided for in the Lord.
 </p><p>III. Is promised in the word.
 </p><p>IV. Is of great value to the believer.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#076">Psa 119:76</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The need of comfort.
 </p><p>II. The source of comfort, "Thy merciful kindness"
 </p><p>III. The rule of comfort, "According to thy word." - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#077">Psa 119:77</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Visitors invited.
 </p><p>II. Boon expected.
 </p><p>III. Welcome guaranteed: "for thy law," etc.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#077">Psa 119:77</a>. - Divine life - it is born, sustained, increased, by God's tender mercies. - W. W.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#078">Psa 119:78</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. A hard thing - to make the proud ashamed.
 </p><p>II. A cruel thing - "they dealt perversely with me," etc.
 </p><p>III. A wise thing - '"but I will meditate," etc.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#079">Psa 119:79</a>. - Restoration to church fellowship.
 </p><p>I. Good men may be in such a case as to need to be restored.
 </p><p>II. They should not be ashamed to seek it.
 </p><p>III. They should pray about it.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#079">Psa 119:79</a>. - Select society.
 </p><p>I. Sociableness is an instinct of human nature.
 </p><p>II. Sociableness is helpful to a wholesome Christian life.
 </p><p>III. The choice of society should be a subject of prayer. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#080">Psa 119:80</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. David's prayer for sincerity - that his heart might be brought to God's statutes, and that it might be sound in them, not rotten or deceitful.
 </p><p>II. His dread of the consequences of hypocrisy, "that I be not ashamed." Shame is the portion of hypocrites, here or hereafter. - M. Henry.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#080">Psa 119:80</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The heart in religion.
 </p><p>II. The necessity of its being sound in it.
 </p><p>III. The result of such sound-heartedness.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#081">Psa 119:81</a>. - Text suitable for a missionary sermon.
 </p><p>I. The condition of the heathen world, enough to make the Christian faint for the salvation of God to visit it.
 </p><p>1. The grossness of its darkness.
 </p><p>2. Its wide area.
 </p><p>3. Its long continuance.
 </p><p>4. The limited character and effort of mission labour.
 </p><p>5. The opposing influences.
 </p><p>II. This condition, though exceedingly sad, is not hopeless. Because - 
 </p><p>1. Of the intention, adaptation, and universal call of the gospel.
 </p><p>2. Of Christ's commission to his church.
 </p><p>3. Of the compassionate character of the spiritually enlightened, produced by their faith in the word.
 </p><p>4. Of the prophecies and promises. Thus, there is hope in the word.
 </p><p>III. If Christians are fainting for the salvation, but hoping in the word, their interest in mission work will be intense, and will show itself.
 </p><p>1. In earnest prayer for more labourers, and greater results.
 </p><p>2. In devoting themselves, if possible, to the work.
 </p><p>3. In free and generous giving, to help on the work. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#081">Psa 119:81</a>. - "My soul fainteth," etc. Men faint for health, provision, rest, promotion, success, and in some instances for salvation. David fainted.
 </p><p>I. For his own salvation.
 </p><p>1. From guilt, "Deliver me from all my transgressions"; "from bloodguiltiness."
 </p><p>2. From defilement, "Create in me a clean heart." "Wash me."
 </p><p>3. From formality, "Let the words of my mouth," etc.
 </p><p>4. From darkness, "Why hidest thou thyself? Lift up," etc. "Say unto my soul," etc.
 </p><p>5. From unhappiness, "Out of the depths," etc.
 </p><p>II. For the salvation of others.
 </p><p>1. He talked about it, "Time for thee to work, Lord."
 </p><p>2. He prayed for it, "Oh that the salvation," etc. "Let thy work," etc. "God be merciful unto us.... Save now, I beseech thee."
 </p><p>3. He laboured for it, "I will make mention of thy righteousness.... I will teach transgressors thy ways." - W. J.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#081">Psa 119:81</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Eagerness expectation.
 </p><p>II. Energy of hope.
 </p><p>III. Establishment of promise, "in thy word."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#081">Psa 119:81</a>. - "Salvation," in Scripture, hath divers acceptations' it is put - 
 </p><p>1. For that temporal deliverance which God giveth, or hath promised to give to his people, so it is taken. <a href="../../../bib/kjv/exo014.htm#013">Exo 14:13</a>.
 </p><p>2. For the exhibition of Christ in the flesh. <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa098.htm#002">Psa 98:2</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa098.htm#003">Psa 98:3</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/luk002.htm#029">Luk 2:29</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/luk002.htm#030">Luk 2:30</a>.
 </p><p>3. For the benefits which we have by Christ on this side of heaven; as the pardon of sin, and the renovation of our natures. <a href="../../../bib/kjv/mat001.htm#021">Mat 1:21</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/tit003.htm#005">Tit 3:5</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa051.htm#012">Psa 51:12</a>.
 </p><p>4. For everlasting life, "Receiving the end of your faith, even the salvation of your souls" (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/pe1001.htm#009">Pe1 1:9</a>); meaning thereby our final reward. - T. Manton.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#081">Psa 119:81</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Faint.
 </p><p>II. Pursuing. - W. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#082">Psa 119:82</a>. - Answer to the enquiry - '"When wilt thou comfort me?"
 </p><p>I. When your grief has answered its purpose.
 </p><p>II. When you believe.
 </p><p>III. When you leave sin.
 </p><p>IV. When you obey.
 </p><p>V. When you submit to my will.
 </p><p>VI. When you seek my glory.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#082">Psa 119:82</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. How longingly the believer turns to God for comfort in his affliction: "When wilt thou comfort me?"
 </p><p>II. How intently he gazes upon the Divine promises, "My eyes fail for thy word."
 </p><p>III. How the weariness of waiting cannot wear out his patience, while hope increases his importunity, "When wilt thou?" - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#082">Psa 119:82</a>. - The pleading of the eyes.
 </p><p>I. How the eyes speak. By "expression" of the moods of the soul, as - longing, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/isa008.htm#017">Isa 8:17</a>; faith, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/isa045.htm#022">Isa 45:22</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/heb012.htm#002">Heb 12:2</a>; expectation, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa005.htm#003">Psa 5:3</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/phi003.htm#020">Phi 3:20</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/tit002.htm#013">Tit 2:13</a>; love, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/co2003.htm#018">Co2 3:18</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/joh001.htm#014">Joh 1:14</a>.
 </p><p>II. What the eyes say. "When wilt thou comfort me? Brushing aside all other comforters, thou art my sun: my life, my love, my all."
 </p><p>III. How the pleading eyes shall meet the responsive Eye of the Lord. <a href="../../../bib/kjv/heb009.htm#018">Heb 9:18</a>. In the look of the recognition of grief, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/exo002.htm#025">Exo 2:25</a>; in the look of pardon, Luke 23:61; of strength-giving, Jud. <a href="../../../bib/kjv/luk006.htm#014">Luk 6:14</a>; of complacent love, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/isa066.htm#002">Isa 66:2</a>. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#083">Psa 119:83</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The outward man in ill case.
 </p><p>II. Character blackened.
 </p><p>III. Constantly exposed to discomfort.
 </p><p>IV. Contents maturing.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#083">Psa 119:83</a>. - "A bottle in the smoke."
 </p><p>I. God's people have their trials.
 </p><p>1. From the poverty of their condition.
 </p><p>2. Our trials frequently result from our comforts.
 </p><p>3. The ministry hath made much smoke with it.
 </p><p>4. The poor bottle in the smoke keeps there for a long time, till it gets black.
 </p><p>II. Christian men feel their troubles; they are like "bottles" in the smoke.
 </p><p>1. The trial that we do not feel is no trial at all.
 </p><p>2. Trials which are not felt are unprofitable trials. A bottle in the smoke gets very black, becomes very useless, is an empty bottle.
 </p><p>III. Christians do not, in their troubles, forget God's statutes of command, the statutes of promise. Why was it that David still held fast by God's statutes?
 </p><p>1. He was not a bottle in the fire, or he would have forgotten them.
 </p><p>2. Jesus Christ was in the smoke with him, and the statutes were in the smoke with him too.
 </p><p>3. The statutes were in the soul, where the smoke does not enter. - From "Spurgeon's Sermons," No. 71.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#084">Psa 119:84</a>. - A solemn question pointing to the shortness of life, the severity of sorrow, the necessity of industry, the nearness of the reward.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#085">Psa 119:85</a>. - Pits; or, the secret schemes of wicked men against the godly.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#086">Psa 119:86</a> (last clause). - A prayer for all occasions. See the many cases in which it is used in Scripture.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#087">Psa 119:87</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. What the good man loses by gaining.
 </p><p>II. What he gains by losing. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#087">Psa 119:87</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. "Almost," but not altogether.
 </p><p>II. The saving clause, "I forsook not thy precepts."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#087">Psa 119:87</a>. - Passing through fires, and the asbestos covering.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#088">Psa 119:88</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. New life is the cause of new obedience.
 </p><p>II. New obedience is the effect of new life. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#088">Psa 119:88</a>. - Quickening.
 </p><p>I. Our greatest need.
 </p><p>II. God's most gracious boon.
 </p><p>III. The guarantee of our steadfastness; and so,
 </p><p>IV. The promoter of God's glory.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#088">Psa 119:88</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. He closes with a frequent petition, "Quicken thou me - make me alive." All true religion consists in the LIFE Of God in the SOUL Of man.
 </p><p>II. The manner in which he wishes to be quickened: "Alter thy lovingkindness." He wishes not to be raised from the death of sin by God's thunder, but by the loving voice of a tender Father.
 </p><p>III. The effect it should have upon him, "So shall I keep the testimony of thy mouth." Whatever thou speakest I will hear, receive, love, and obey. - Adam Clarke.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#089">Psa 119:89-92</a>. - The Psalmist here tells us the prescription which soothed his pains and sustained his spirits. Here we have strong consolation.
 </p><p>I. In certain facts which he remembered.
 </p><p>1. The eternal existence of God.
 </p><p>2. The immutability of his word.
 </p><p>3. The faithfulness of the fulfilment of that word.
 </p><p>4. The perpetuity of the word in nature.
 </p><p>5. The perpetuity of the word in experience.
 </p><p>II. The delights which he experienced in the time of his trouble. In bereavements; when everything seemed shifting and inconstant; when his own faith failed him; when all helpers failed him; he fell back upon the eternal settlements, "O Lord, thy word is settled," etc. See "Spurgeon's Sermons," No. 1656, "My Solace in my Affliction."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#089">Psa 119:89</a>. - Eternal settlements, or, heavenly certainties.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#089">Psa 119:89</a>. - God's eternal calm (in contrast with earth's mutations) imaged in the starry heavens. - William Bickle Haynes, 1882.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#089">Psa 119:89</a>. - Consider,
 </p><p>I. The term, "thy word."
 </p><p>1. A word is a revealed thought. The Scriptures are just this' the thoughts and purposes of God made intelligible to man.
 </p><p>2. But a "word" also marks specially unity (it is one word) and wholeness or completeness, a word, not a syllable. The Scriptures are one and complete.
 </p><p>II. The statement, "for ever settled in heaven."
 </p><p>1. "Settled in heaven" before it came to earth; therefore it could come as a continuous unfolding, through various dispensations, without the shadow of hesitation or contradiction manifest in it.
 </p><p>2. Abides "settled in heaven," for its central revelation; the atonement is a completed fact, and Christ is now in heaven a perfected Saviour; thus the word is unalterable.
 </p><p>3. "For ever settled in heaven." Not only because God in heaven is of one mind and cannot be turned; but because righteousness itself, the righteousness of heaven, demands that an atonement by suffering shall be fully and ever - lastingly answered by its due reward.
 </p><p>III. The lessons.
 </p><p>1. If settled in heaven, men on earth can never unsettle it.
 </p><p>2. The wicked may not indulge a future hope arising from any new dispensation beyond the grave; God's present word to us cannot then be unsettled.
 </p><p>3. The godly may rely on a settled word amidst the unsettled experiences and feelings incident to earth. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#090">Psa 119:90</a>. - The stability of the earth a present picture of everlasting faithfulness.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#090">Psa 119:90</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#091">Psa 119:91</a>. - Consider,
 </p><p>I. The steadfastness of nature as dependent upon the divine decree, "according to thy ordinances."
 </p><p>II. The subserviency of nature to the divine will, "for all are thy servants."
 </p><p>III. The fixedness of nature's laws, together with their subserviency to God's purposes, as a confirmation of the Christian's faith in the written word, in the care of a divine providence, and in the sureness of spiritual and heavenly things. "Thy faithfulness is," etc. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#091">Psa 119:91</a>. - Our starry monitors. They teach us,
 </p><p>I. To serve, though we cannot shine with their brightness.
 </p><p>II. To do all with strict regard to God's will.
 </p><p>III. To "continue" - "according to thine ordinances." W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#091">Psa 119:91</a>. - The service of nature.
 </p><p>I. Universal, "all are thy servants."
 </p><p>II. Obedient, "according to thy ordinances"
 </p><p>III. Perpetual, "they continue."
 </p><p>IV. Derived, "thou hast established the earth."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#092">Psa 119:92</a>. - The sustaining power of joy in God.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#092">Psa 119:92</a>. - The word of God as a sustaining power amid the greater sorrows of life.
 </p><p>I. Its necessity.
 </p><p>1. For want of it, men have become drunkards to drown their sorrows, have become suicides because life was unbearable, have become broken and hopeless because they had no strength to struggle against misfortune, have become atheists in creed as, alas, they were before in practice; all, in fact, become subject to sorrow's worst bitterness and calamity's worst effects.
 </p><p>2. Nothing can supply the place of God's word. Nature throws no light on the mystery of suffering. Human philosophy is at best cold comfort, and when most needed most fails.
 </p><p>II. Its efficiency. Proved - 
 </p><p>1. In the experience of those who have tried it.
 </p><p>2. By the character of its promises.
 </p><p>3. By the discovery it makes of a beneficent providence working through calamity and sorrow.
 </p><p>4. By the revelation it gives of the pity of God and the sympathy of Christ.
 </p><p>5. By its record of the "Man of sorrows," who through suffering wrought out man's salvation, and entered into glory.
 </p><p>6. By its teaching concerning the Incarnate Word; thus showing a suffering God, which may well be a solace to suffering meal.
 </p><p>7. By displaying the glory of heaven and the eternal felicity awaiting those who overcome through the blood of the Lamb. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#092">Psa 119:92</a>. - The Godly Man's Ark; or, City of Refuge in the day of his Distress. Discovered in divers (five) Sermons By Edmund Calamy, B. D. Eighteenth edition. 1709. 12mo.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#092">Psa 119:92</a>. - We have here set before us by the Psalmist,
 </p><p>I. The case which he had been in, and which he now refers to - one sad and sinking. He was under such affliction that he was ready to perish; which seems to include inward and outward trouble at once; trials without and pressure within.
 </p><p>II. What it was that gave him relief, and this when nothing else could, etc., the law of God.
 </p><p>III. How he looked back upon this relief received, namely, with thankfulness to God, to whom he speaks, and records it for the encouragement and direction of others: "Unless thy law had been my delights, I should then have perished in mine affliction." - Daniel Wilcox, 1676 -1733.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#092">Psa 119:92</a>. - The life-buoy. Under the form of the narrative of a shipwrecked mariner, describe the experience of the soul struggling in the sea of affliction; almost overwhelmed: yet buoyed up over each successive billow: and finally saved by clinging to the Word of God. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#092">Psa 119:92</a>. - The Psalmist's shudder at recollected danger.
 </p><p>I. Sore peril: affliction tending to despair and ruin.
 </p><p>II. Fearful crisis, "then."
 </p><p>III. Many-handed help: "thy law my delights." - W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#093">Psa 119:93</a>. - Experience fixes the word upon the memory.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#093">Psa 119:93</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. A good resolve: "I will never forget thy precepts."
 </p><p>1. The precepts are worth remembering.
 </p><p>2. Safety lies in remembering them.
 </p><p>3. Fidelity to God cannot be without remembering them.
 </p><p>4. Not to remember them is shameful ingratitude.
 </p><p>II. An excellent reason for making it: "For with them thou hast quickened me."
 </p><p>1. A reason founded upon personal experience, "me."
 </p><p>2. A reason appreciative of the benefit received: "quickened."
 </p><p>3. A reason indicative of gratitude to God: "thou." - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#093">Psa 119:93</a>. - "Never forget"; an often-uttered phrase. Here golden.
 </p><p>I. Something that could not be forgotten, life and pardon received. How could it?
 </p><p>II. Something that should not be forgotten: the precious instrumentality. - W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#093">Psa 119:93</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The instrumental power of truth.
 </p><p>1. Used by God in our regeneration, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/jam001.htm#018">Jam 1:18</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa019.htm#007">Psa 19:7</a>.
 </p><p>2. Used in our liberation, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/joh008.htm#032">Joh 8:32</a>.
 </p><p>3. Used in our sanctification - <a href="../../../bib/kjv/joh017.htm#007">Joh 17:7</a>.
 </p><p>II. Our consequent affection for it. We cannot forget.
 </p><p>1. Our past obligations to it.
 </p><p>2. Our present dependence upon it.
 </p><p>3. Our future needs of it. - W. W.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#094">Psa 119:94</a>. - 
 </p><p>1. David claims relation to God: "I am thine" - devoted to thee, and owned by thee, thine in covenant.
 </p><p>2. He proves his claim: "I am thine, save me; for I have sought thy precepts"; i.e., I have carefully enquired concerning my duty, and diligently endeavoured to do it.
 </p><p>3. He improves his claim: "I am thine, save me." Save me from sin, save me from ruin. - M. Henry.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#094">Psa 119:94</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. A great prayer: "Save me."
 </p><p>II. A grand prayer: "I am thine."
 </p><p>III. A gracious experience: "I have sought," etc.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#094">Psa 119:94</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Relation: "I am thine."
 </p><p>II. Preservation: "save me."
 </p><p>III. Obligation: "I have sought," etc. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#094">Psa 119:94</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. God's child humbly points out to him his responsibility: "I am thine."
 </p><p>II. Ventures to urge his own sincerity, he has at least "sought."
 </p><p>III. With these two hands extended, he utters a sharp cry for help, "save me." - W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#094">Psa 119:94</a>. - Multum in parvo.
 </p><p>I. A profession.
 </p><p>II. A prayer.
 </p><p>III. A plea. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#094">Psa 119:94</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. God's interest in us.
 </p><p>II. Our interest in God. - W. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#094">Psa 119:94</a>. - The characteristics of personal religion.
 </p><p>I. Personal devotedness to God: "I am thine."
 </p><p>II. Personal obedience rendered: "I have sought thy precepts."
 </p><p>III. Personal expectation cherished; "save me." - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#094">Psa 119:94</a>. - The courage obedience gives.
 </p><p>I. It emboldens us to a firm assurance: "I am thine, for I have," etc.
 </p><p>1. We become God's by faith alone.
 </p><p>2. But the assurance of being his cannot exist without obedience; obedience proves the faith to ourselves; satisfies us concerning grace received.
 </p><p>3. Poor obedience always interferes with assurance.
 </p><p>II. It emboldens us to pray, and in prayer - "Save me."
 </p><p>1. The Christian's prayers are only of faith and offered in faith.
 </p><p>2. Yet disobedience makes him shrink from approaching God in prayer, and renders him feeble in petitioning.
 </p><p>3. Obedience is humble but bold. The middle clause of the text applies equally to the first and third clauses. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#095">Psa 119:95</a>. - Wicked men patient in carrying out their evil designs. Good men patient in considering the ways of the Lord.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#095">Psa 119:95</a>. - The hatred of the wicked towards the righteous.
 </p><p>I. Show that it ever has been, and still is.
 </p><p>1. Select Scriptural instances, beginning with Abel.
 </p><p>2. Notice the persecutions of the church.
 </p><p>3. Treatment in the workshop.
 </p><p>4. Often in the home.
 </p><p>5. The contemptuous manner the "saints" are spoken of, etc.
 </p><p>II. Enquire as to why it is so.
 </p><p>1. The enmity of the carnal heart to God.
 </p><p>2. The jealousy excited by the Christian's assurance of eternal blessedness.
 </p><p>3. The consciousness of being rebuked by a holy life.
 </p><p>4. Excited to it by Satan.
 </p><p>5. The restless mischievousness of sin which, if it cannot hinder holiness, will maliciously hurt its advocates.
 </p><p>III. Direct how to act when exposed to it: "I will consider thy testimonies." That means - 
 </p><p>1. Be the more obedient to God.
 </p><p>2. Have the more watchful control over words and feelings.
 </p><p>3. Love your enemies.
 </p><p>4. Pray for those who hate you.
 </p><p>5. Do good to them on every opportunity.
 </p><p>6. Be thankful that you are among the hated and not the haters.
 </p><p>7. Especially consider the holy testimony of Christ's forbearing patience. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#095">Psa 119:95</a>. - Waiting counterwrought by waiting.
 </p><p>I. Temptations in ambush.
 </p><p>II. The saint with his Lord. - W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#095">Psa 119:95</a>. - Immunity.
 </p><p>I. I am in danger.
 </p><p>II. I will attend to my duty.
 </p><p>III. I will trust thee to deliver me. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#096">Psa 119:96</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. An end: - "seen"; seen by one man where it should not have been; seen where there was no end of boasting; seen in all perfection.
 </p><p>II. No end: - to the extent, spirituality, perpetuity, and perfectness of the law.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#096">Psa 119:96</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The Finite explored.
 </p><p>II. The Infinite unexplored. W. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#096">Psa 119:96</a>. - Perfectionism disproved by experience and inspiration. - W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#096">Psa 119:96</a>. - Perfection - perfect and imperfect.
 </p><p>I. Loud professions of perfection arise from ignorance (of self, or of God's requirements).
 </p><p>II. Are peculiarly liable to collapse, "I have seen an end."
 </p><p>III. Are best corrected by a survey of the breadth of the divine law. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#097">Psa 119:97</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Unusual Exclamation.
 </p><p>II. Unusual Application. - W. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#097">Psa 119:97</a>. - Indescribable love and insatiable thought. The action and reaction of affection and meditation.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#097">Psa 119:97</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The object of love, "thy law."
 </p><p>II. The degree of that love, "oh, how love I," etc.
 </p><p>III. The evidence of that love, "it is my meditation," etc. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#097">Psa 119:97</a>. - Love to the law.
 </p><p>I. An ardent confession of love.
 </p><p>II. An unanswerable evidence of love. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#097">Psa 119:97</a> (first clause). - Vehemency of love for God's word.
 </p><p>I. Its recognisable marks.
 </p><p>1. Profound reverence for the authority of the word.
 </p><p>2. Admiration for its holiness.
 </p><p>3. Jealousy for its honour; God's servant feels acute pain when men show it any slight.
 </p><p>4. Respect for its wholeness; he would not divorce precepts from promises, nor ignore a single statement in it.
 </p><p>5. Indefatigability in its study.
 </p><p>6. Eager desire to obey it.
 </p><p>7. Forwardness in praising it.
 </p><p>8. Activity in spreading it abroad.
 </p><p>II. Its reasonableness.
 </p><p>1. The word well deserves it.
 </p><p>2. It is a proof of true intelligence.
 </p><p>3. It is not less than a regard for our own interest demands.
 </p><p>III. Its requisiteness to the true worship of God. Men sneeringly call such an affection bibliolatry, as though it were the worship of a book. In truth, it is an essential element in the due worship of God. For - 
 </p><p>1. Without it there cannot be the faith which honours God.
 </p><p>2. It is involved in that love to God which constitutes the very essence of worship.
 </p><p>3. It is itself an act of homage that a worshipper dare not withhold. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#097">Psa 119:97-100</a>. - Spiritual wisdom.
 </p><p>I. God's word the source of surpassing wisdom - excelling that of "mine enemies," "my teachers," "the ancients."
 </p><p>II. The three methods of acquiring this wisdom - love, meditation, practice.
 </p><p>III. The one Giver of this wisdom, "Thou'" <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#098">Psa 119:98</a>. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#098">Psa 119:98</a>. - Constant communion with truth the student's road to proficiency.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#098">Psa 119:98</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#099">Psa 119:99</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#100">Psa 119:100</a>. - The truly wise man.
 </p><p>1. The source of his wisdom. The word of "the only wise God," here described as
 </p><p>(1) Thy commandments.
 </p><p>(2) Thy testimonies.
 </p><p>(3) Thy precepts.
 </p><p>2. The increase of his wisdom. It arises from
 </p><p>(1) The abiding indwelling of the word, "ever with me," <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#098">Psa 119:98</a>.
 </p><p>(2) Meditation upon the word, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#099">Psa 119:99</a>.
 </p><p>(3) Obedience to the word, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#100">Psa 119:100</a>.
 </p><p>3. The measure of his wisdom.
 </p><p>(1) Wiser than his enemies, whose wisdom was "not from above, but earthly, sensual, devilish."
 </p><p>(2) Wiser than his teachers, whose wisdom was "of this world."
 </p><p>(3) Wiser than the ancients, whose wisdom was that of unsanctified age and experience. - W. H. J. Page, 1882.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#099">Psa 119:99</a>. - The surest way to excellence.
 </p><p>I. A good subject: "thy testimonies."
 </p><p>II. A good method, "are my meditations."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#100">Psa 119:100</a>. - Antiquity no security for truth as contrasted with revelation, old age no proof of wisdom as contrasted with holy living, open confession no evidence of boasting as contrasted with sullen pride.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#100">Psa 119:100</a>. - Obedience the high road to understanding. - W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#100">Psa 119:100</a>. - Obedience the key of knowledge. <a href="../../../bib/kjv/joh007.htm#017">Joh 7:17</a>.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#100">Psa 119:100</a>. - Self-restraint needful to piety.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#102">Psa 119:102</a>. - Divine teaching necessary to secure perseverance, and effectual to that end.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#102">Psa 119:102</a>. - Consider. - 
 </p><p>I. The path appointed for men to walk in: "Thy judgments."
 </p><p>1. Bight path.
 </p><p>2. Clean path.
 </p><p>3. Pleasant path.
 </p><p>4. Safe path.
 </p><p>5. The end - eternal glory.
 </p><p>II. The persistent pursuit of it: "I have not departed."
 </p><p>1. Persecution would drive from it.
 </p><p>2. Pleasures would allure from it.
 </p><p>3. The flesh would weary in it.
 </p><p>4. But the true believer determines to hold on his way to the end.
 </p><p>5. And carefully watches his steps lest they depart.
 </p><p>III. The preserving power that holds the traveller to it, "For thou hast taught me."
 </p><p>1. The traveller walks with God, and receives instruction by the special illumination of the Holy Spirit.
 </p><p>2. The choice property of this teaching is, not only that it makes wise, but that it captivates the soul, strengthens it, and holds it to a holy obedience. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#103">Psa 119:103</a>. - Experience in religion the source of enjoyment in it; or,
 </p><p>I. Tasting the word - its sweetness.
 </p><p>II. Declaring the word with the mouth its greater sweetness.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#103">Psa 119:103</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The word is positively sweet - "sweet to my taste."
 </p><p>II. Comparatively sweet - "sweeter than honey."
 </p><p>III. Superlatively sweet, "how sweet," etc. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#103">Psa 119:103</a>. - The comparison, setting forth the precious property of sweetness in the word, "Sweeter than honey." "Better than honey," would not do as well. It is - 
 </p><p>I. The purest sweetness; even precepts and rebukes.
 </p><p>II. Uncloying sweetness.
 </p><p>III. Always a beneficial sweetness.
 </p><p>IV. A specially grateful sweetness - in affliction, in the hour of death. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#103">Psa 119:103</a>. - Spiritual delicacy.
 </p><p>I. The taste needed to relish it.
 </p><p>II. The life that alone is nourished by it.
 </p><p>III. The rare enjoyment derived from it. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#103">Psa 119:103</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. It is sweet.
 </p><p>II. Let us enjoy it.
 </p><p>III. The best effects will follow. George Herbert says: - 
 </p><p>"O Book! infinite sweetness! let my heart
 </p><p>Suck every letter, and a honey gain,
 </p><p>Precious for any grief in any part;
 </p><p>To clear the breast, to mollify all pain."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#103">Psa 119:103</a>. - If we would taste the honey of God, we must have the palate of faith. - A. R. Faussett.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#104">Psa 119:104</a>. - The influence of the precepts.
 </p><p>I. Upon the understanding.
 </p><p>II. Upon the affections.
 </p><p>III. Upon the life.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#104">Psa 119:104</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The intellectual effect of the Scriptures. "I get understanding."
 </p><p>II. Their moral effect, "I hate," etc. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#104">Psa 119:104</a>. - The understanding derived from God's precepts begets holy hatred,
 </p><p>I. To the false ways of conventional morality.
 </p><p>II. To the false ways of a formal religiousness.
 </p><p>III. To the false ways of an erring theology.
 </p><p>IV. To the false ways of hypocritical practice.
 </p><p>V. To the false ways of sinful suggestions.
 </p><p>VI. To the false ways of one's own deceitful heart. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#105">Psa 119:105-108</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Illumination (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#105">Psa 119:105</a>).
 </p><p>II. Decision (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#106">Psa 119:106</a>).
 </p><p>III. Testing, "I am afflicted" (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#107">Psa 119:107</a>).
 </p><p>IV. Consecration (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#108">Psa 119:108</a>).
 </p><p>V. Education, "teach me," etc. (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#108">Psa 119:108</a>).
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#105">Psa 119:105</a>. - The practical, personal, everyday use of the word of God.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#105">Psa 119:105</a>. - Lamp-light.
 </p><p>I. The believer's dangerous night-journey through the world.
 </p><p>II. The lamp that illumines his path.
 </p><p>III. The eternal day towards which he travels (when the lamp will be laid aside. <a href="../../../bib/kjv/rev022.htm#005">Rev 22:5</a>). - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#106">Psa 119:106</a>. - Decision for God, and fit modes of expressing it.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#106">Psa 119:106</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Veneration for the word.
 </p><p>II. Consecration to the word.
 </p><p>III. Fidelity to the word. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#106">Psa 119:106</a>. - Swearing and performing.
 </p><p>I. The usefulness of religious vows. To quicken perception; to rouse conscience; (seen in Jewish nation - <a href="../../../bib/kjv/exo024.htm#003">Exo 24:3-7</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/ch2015.htm#012">Ch2 15:12-15</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/neh010.htm#028">Neh 10:28</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/neh010.htm#029">Neh 10:29</a>; in Scottish nation - Solemn League and Covenant).
 </p><p>II. The danger of religious vows. A vow unfulfilled, or receded from, is a moral injury: <a href="../../../bib/kjv/ecc005.htm#004">Ecc 5:4-7</a>.
 </p><p>III. The safeguard of religious vows: dependence on the Spirit of God - <a href="../../../bib/kjv/eze011.htm#019">Eze 11:19</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/eze011.htm#020">Eze 11:20</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/co2004.htm#005">Co2 4:5</a>. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#107">Psa 119:107</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. A good man greatly afflicted.
 </p><p>II. A sure cure for the ills of affliction: "Quicken me."
 </p><p>III. A safe rule to pray by when afflicted. "according unto thy word."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#107">Psa 119:107</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The "very much" afflicted.
 </p><p>1. The world has such - widows, orphans, ere, etc.
 </p><p>2. Most take their turn.
 </p><p>II. But there is "very much" grace.
 </p><p>1. God's word promises the needed quickening.
 </p><p>2. Himself very much greater than all our needs.
 </p><p>3. Christ tried "in all points" has all help.
 </p><p>III. Therefore bring "very much" faith, as the Psalmist here.
 </p><p>1. Keen-eyed for promises.
 </p><p>2. Fervent in pleading them.
 </p><p>3. Strong in expectation. - W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#108">Psa 119:108</a>. - Consider, - 
 </p><p>I. The instructive title given to prayer and praise, "The free-will offerings of my mouth."
 </p><p>1. It shows the believer to be a priest, "offerings."
 </p><p>2. It shows the peculiarity of his service: "free-will."
 </p><p>3. It implies whole-hearted consecration.
 </p><p>II. The humility portrayed in the prayer, "Accept, I beseech thee."
 </p><p>1. Here is no pharisaic boasting.
 </p><p>2. Even the free-will offering is felt to need an "I beseech thee."
 </p><p>III. The longing desire for further instruction in order to a more perfect obedience: "Teach me thy judgments." - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#108">Psa 119:108</a>. - Free will seeking free grace. - W. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#108">Psa 119:108</a>. - Work for Free-willers.
 </p><p>I. Offerings of Prayers for each of the blessings of salvation.
 </p><p>II. Offerings of Repudiation of all claim to unassisted good.
 </p><p>III. Offerings of Praise - for sovereign grace. - W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#109">Psa 119:109</a>. - The soul's life in jeopardy. The life of the soul secured.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#109">Psa 119:109</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#110">Psa 119:110</a>. - Here is - 
 </p><p>I. David in danger of losing his life. There is but a step between him and death, for "the wicked have laid a snare" for him. Wherever he was he found some design or other laid against him; which made him say, "My soul is continually in my hand." It was not so only as a man - it is true of us all that we are exposed to the strokes of death - but as a man of war, and especially as "a man after God's own heart."
 </p><p>II. David in no danger of losing his religion through this peril; for,
 </p><p>1. He "doth not forget the law," and therefore is likely to persevere.
 </p><p>2. He hath not yet erred from God's precepts, and therefore it is to be hoped he will not. - M. Henry.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#110">Psa 119:110</a>. - Various kinds of snares, and the one way of escaping them.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#110">Psa 119:110</a>. - Consider, - 
 </p><p>I. Some of the snares set for saints by sinners.
 </p><p>1. Doctrinal snares, by intellectual sinners.
 </p><p>2. False accusations, by malignant sinners.
 </p><p>3. False flatteries, by deceitful sinners.
 </p><p>4. False charity, by a large number of sinners nowadays.
 </p><p>III. The secure safeguard for a saint's safety, "I erred not from thy precepts." Obedience to God gives security, because - 
 </p><p>1. The snares are then suspected and watched against.
 </p><p>2. The feet cannot become entangled by them.
 </p><p>3. God keeps him who keeps his word. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#111">Psa 119:111</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Estate.
 </p><p>II. Entering upon it.
 </p><p>III. Entail upon it.
 </p><p>IV. Enjoyment of it.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#111">Psa 119:111</a>. - Notice, - 
 </p><p>I. How rich the Psalmist - as determined to be, "Thy testimonies have I taken as a heritage." Rich, - 
 </p><p>1. In knowledge.
 </p><p>2. In holiness.
 </p><p>3. In comfort.
 </p><p>4. In companionship, for God's company goes with his word.
 </p><p>5. In hope.
 </p><p>II. How he clung to his wealth, "For ever."
 </p><p>1. He hurt none by so doing; he could give generously his portion, and yet not waste.
 </p><p>2. He was right; for he had the only wealth of which an everlasting possession is possible.
 </p><p>3. He was wise.
 </p><p>III. How he rejoiced in his wealth, "They are the rejoicing of my heart."
 </p><p>1. Here is internal and deep joy; not always possible to the possession of wealth.
 </p><p>2. Pure, unalloyed joy; it is never so with other wealth.
 </p><p>3. Safe joy; other joy is dangerous.
 </p><p>4. Unlosable joy. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#112">Psa 119:112</a>. - Heart-leanings. Personality, pressure, inclination, performance, constancy, perpetuity.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#112">Psa 119:112</a>. - The godly man's obedience.
 </p><p>I. Its reality.
 </p><p>1. "To perform"; not words or feelings merely; but deeds.
 </p><p>2. "Thy statutes"; not human inventions, nor self conceits, nor conventional maxims.
 </p><p>II. Its cordiality, "inclined my heart."
 </p><p>1. Heart inclination is requisite for pleasing a heart-searching God.
 </p><p>2. And to make obedience easy and even delightful.
 </p><p>3. "I have," he says; was it therefore his doing? Yes. Was it his work alone? No. See <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#036">Psa 119:36</a>.
 </p><p>4. The proofs.
 </p><p>(1) Universality: "statutes," the whole of them.
 </p><p>(2) Uniformity: "alway."
 </p><p>III. Its constancy, "even unto the end."
 </p><p>1. Though a man should be cautious when planning for the future, yet this life-long purpose is right, wise, and safe.
 </p><p>2. Nor can he purpose less, if holy fervency fill the heart.
 </p><p>3. It is no more than what God and consistency demand. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#113">Psa 119:113</a>. - The thought of the age, and the truth of all ages.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#113">Psa 119:113</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The object of hatred.
 </p><p>II. The object of love.
 </p><p>Or - 
 </p><p>I. Love the cause of hatred.
 </p><p>II. Hatred the effect of love. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#113">Psa 119:113</a>. - "Vain thoughts." What they are. Whence they arise. The mischief they cause. How they should be treated. - W. H. J. P.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#113">Psa 119:113</a>. - How the believer - 
 </p><p>I. Is troubled by vain thoughts. A frequent and painful experience.
 </p><p>II. Does not tolerate vain thoughts. Some suffer them to lodge within; he is anxious to expel them.
 </p><p>III. Triumphs over vain thoughts. By his love to the law of God. His prayer is - 
 </p><p>"With thoughts of Christ and things divine,
 </p><p>Fill up this foolish heart of mine."
 </p><p>W. H. J. P.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#114">Psa 119:114</a>. - Our protection from danger - '"hiding-place"; in danger "shield"; be fore danger - '"I hope."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#114">Psa 119:114</a>. - "Hiding-place." Secrecy to conceal us. Capacity to hold us. Safety. Comfort. - T. Manton.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#114">Psa 119:114</a>. - Hiding and hoping.
 </p><p>I. A hiding-place needed.
 </p><p>II. A hiding-place provided (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/isa025.htm#001">Isa 25:1-4</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/isa032.htm#002">Isa 32:2</a>).
 </p><p>III. A hiding-place used. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#114">Psa 119:114</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The refuge provided: "Thou art," etc.
 </p><p>II. The refuge revealed: "In thy word."
 </p><p>III. The refuge found, "I hope," etc. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#114">Psa 119:114</a>. - "Thou art my hiding place."
 </p><p>I. In thy grace, from condemnation.
 </p><p>II. In thy compassion, from sorrow.
 </p><p>III. In thy succour, from temptation.
 </p><p>IV. In thy power, from opposition.
 </p><p>V. In thy fulness, from want. - W. J.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#115">Psa 119:115</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Ill company hinders piety.
 </p><p>II. Piety quits ill company.
 </p><p>III. Piety, in compelling this departure, acts as God will do at the last.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#115">Psa 119:115</a>. - Evil companionships incompatible with genuine righteousness.
 </p><p>I. They necessitate concealment and compromise.
 </p><p>II. They destroy the capability of communion with God, and the relish for spiritual things.
 </p><p>III. They blunt the sensitiveness of conscience.
 </p><p>IV. They involve deliberate disobedience to God. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#116">Psa 119:116</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Upholding promised.
 </p><p>II. Needful for holy living.
 </p><p>III. The preventive of shameful acts.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#116">Psa 119:116</a>. - "Uphold me according unto thy word," etc.
 </p><p>I. The Psalmist pleads the promise of God, his dependence upon the promise, and his expectation from it: "Uphold me according unto thy word," which word I hope in, and if it be not performed I shall be "ashamed of my hope."
 </p><p>II. He pleads the great need he had of God's grace, and the great advantage it would be to him: "Uphold me, that I may live"; intimating that he could not live without the grace of God. - M. Henry.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#117">Psa 119:117</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Upholding - God's holding us up. It implies a danger, and that danger takes many forms. The believer's life, nay be described as walking in uprightness; he is a pilgrim. He needs upholding, for - 
 </p><p>1. The way is slippery.
 </p><p>2. Our feet make the danger as well as the way.
 </p><p>3. Cunning foes seek to trip us up.
 </p><p>4. Sometimes the difficulty is not caused by the way, but by the height to which God may elevate us.
 </p><p>5. The prayer is all the more needful because the most of people do not keep upright.
 </p><p>II. Two blessed things that come out of this holding up.
 </p><p>1. We shall be safe for ourselves, as examples, and as pillars of the church.
 </p><p>2. We shall be watchful and sensitive. "I will have respect unto thy statutes continually." Without this no man is safe. See "Spurgeon's Sermons," No. 1657, "My Hourly Prayer."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#117">Psa 119:117</a>. - "Hold thou me up," etc.
 </p><p>I. The good man is up.
 </p><p>II. The good man wishes to keep up.
 </p><p>III. The good man prays to be held up.
 </p><p>IV. The good man knows that divine support is abundantly sufficient. - W. J.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#117">Psa 119:117</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Dependence for the future: "Hold," etc.
 </p><p>II. Resolution for the future: "I will have," etc. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#118">Psa 119:118</a>. - Sin and falsehood: their connection, punishment, and cure.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#118">Psa 119:118</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Hearken to the tramp of God's armies. In nature; providence; angelic hosts of last day.
 </p><p>II. The mangled victims. Cunning deceivers specially obnoxious to God. Examples. Balaam, Pharaoh, Rome, the deceiver of the nations.
 </p><p>III. The warnings to us of this Aceldama. Repent. Avoid deceit. Mind God's landmarks. Hide in Christ. - W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#118">Psa 119:118</a>. - God's punishment of the wicked though awfully severe is just and necessary.
 </p><p>I. It is due as the merited wages of iniquity.
 </p><p>II. It is demanded by the position of God as moral governor, and by his character as righteous.
 </p><p>III. It is necessary to mark the real worth of righteousness and its reward. If the wicked are not punished, the full worth of righteousness cannot appear.
 </p><p>IV. In the nature of the case, it is absolutely unavoidable, except upon one condition, namely, the gift of genuine repentance and holiness after death; that no man has any right to expect, nor has God given the slightest intimation that he will bestow it.
 </p><p>V. Hell lies in the bosom of sin; and if the wicked were taken to heaven, they would carry hell thither. Heaven supplies not the things in which the wicked delight while it abounds in those they can neither understand nor sympathise with. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#118">Psa 119:118</a> (second clause). - The deceits of the wicked are all falsehoods.
 </p><p>I. The world they embrace is a false Delilah.
 </p><p>II. The pleasure they enjoy is a Satanic snare.
 </p><p>III. Their formal religiousness is a vain delusion.
 </p><p>IV. Their conceits of God are self-invented lies. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#118">Psa 119:118-120</a>. - Saved by fear.
 </p><p>I. The wrath of God revealed against sin.
 </p><p>II. The judgment of God executed upon sinners.
 </p><p>III. The fear of God created in the heart. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#119">Psa 119:119</a>. - The saint's acquiescence in God's judgments. - W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#119">Psa 119:119</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Comparison of the wicked to dross.
 </p><p>II. Comparison of their doom to the putting away of dross.
 </p><p>III. The saint's admiration of divine justice as seen in the rejection of the wicked.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#119">Psa 119:119</a>. - God's putting away the wicked like dross.
 </p><p>I. God's judgments are a searching and separating fire.
 </p><p>II. The final judgment of the great day will complete the separating process.
 </p><p>III. The great result will be, the true metal and the dross, each gathered to its own place. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#120">Psa 119:120</a>. - The judgments of God on the wicked cause in the righteous,
 </p><p>I. Love.
 </p><p>II. Awe.
 </p><p>III. Fear.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#120">Psa 119:120</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Describe the true character of the fear.
 </p><p>1. It is the fear of reverence for God's authority and power.
 </p><p>2. It is the fear of horror against sin as meriting judgment.
 </p><p>II. Show its compatibility with filial love.
 </p><p>1. The more we love God the more firmly we believe in the certainty and awfulness of his judgments.
 </p><p>2. The more we love God the more will we fear to arouse his chastising rod against ourselves.
 </p><p>3. In fact, if we love not God, we shall have no fear lest sin should involve us in judgment.
 </p><p>III. Commend it.
 </p><p>1. As it proves a just sense of sin's desert.
 </p><p>2. As it shows a true appreciation of God's righteousness.
 </p><p>3. As it is not a fear that hath torment, but a fear which increases watchfulness, and walks hand in hand with perfect confidence in saving grace. - d. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#121">Psa 119:121</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#122">Psa 119:122</a>. - The double appeal.
 </p><p>I. Of conscious integrity: "I have done judgment," etc.
 </p><p>II. Of conscious deficiency, "Be surety for thy servant for good." - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#122">Psa 119:122</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Suretyship entreated.
 </p><p>II. Good expected.
 </p><p>III. Obligation acknowledged: "thy servant."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#122">Psa 119:122</a> (first clause). - After explaining the Psalmist's meaning as shown in <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#121">Psa 119:121</a>, this sentence nay be used for a sermon upon the Suretyship of Christ, by a reference to <a href="../../../bib/kjv/heb007.htm#022">Heb 7:22</a>.
 </p><p>I. A Surety for good wanted - the deeply felt, though, perhaps, undefined want of a sin-burdened soul.
 </p><p>1. The mere statement of a gratuitous pardon on the part of God is not thoroughly believable to such a soul, nor, if it could be believed in, would it give peace to the conscience. For, on the one hand, the pardon could not be perceived as just, nor as consistent with God's necessary hatred of sin, yet the conscience demands this perception; on the other hand, mere pardon does not show how the obligation to a perfect fulfilment of God's law, as righteousness, can be met, yet the conscience demands to see this before it can be satisfied to realize peace. Luther's experience.
 </p><p>2. Now the Scriptures tell us that God "justifies the ungodly," and that his "righteousness" is declared in his justifying sinners - <a href="../../../bib/kjv/rom003.htm#025">Rom 3:25</a>. He can forgive sins with justice. He can treat sinners as righteous persons, and yet be righteous in doing so. How? By a Surety. Therefore, a Surety is the real want.
 </p><p>II. A Surety existent. Jesus is the Surety.
 </p><p>1. He undertook to bear our obligation to the law's penalty, and fulfilled it in death. Thus pardon, though mercy to us, is an act of justice to Christ.
 </p><p>2. He undertook our obligation to a perfect obedience, and satisfied for that in his fulfilment of the law; thus for God to treat us as righteous is only just to Christ.
 </p><p>3. God has shown his satisfaction with the office of Christ, and with his work, by the resurrection and glorification of Christ. Hence a well-accredited and efficient Surety exists.
 </p><p>III. A Surety nigh at hand.
 </p><p>1. In the gospel, Christ as Surety comes to the sinner as truly as though he himself left his throne and came in his own person.
 </p><p>2. Thus, he is so close that a sinner has but to receive the gospel into his heart and he receives Christ.
 </p><p>3. Christ received as a Surety is the Surety for whosoever receives him. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#123">Psa 119:123</a>. - Holy expectation - long maintained, in danger of failing; this fact pleaded; reasons for never renouncing it.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#124">Psa 119:124</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#125">Psa 119:125</a>. - The servant of God.
 </p><p>I. Making profession, "I am thy servant."
 </p><p>II. Making confession - of guilt, dulness, ignorance.
 </p><p>III. Making petition - for mercy, understanding, and teaching. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#124">Psa 119:124</a>. - Heavenly instruction a great mercy.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#124">Psa 119:124</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. His confidence in divine mercy.
 </p><p>II. His submission to divine authority.
 </p><p>III. His prayer for divine teaching. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#124">Psa 119:124</a>. - A Perfect Prayer,
 </p><p>I. As to the matter of it.
 </p><p>1. Here is nothing superfluous; no petition for wealth, nor for honours, nor for anything the worldling covets.
 </p><p>2. Here is nothing wanting; "Deal with thy servant according to thy mercy" comprehends everything the guilty soul needs; "Teach me thy statutes" comprehends all a saint needs to be anxious for.
 </p><p>II. As to the manner of it.
 </p><p>1. It is direct and definite.
 </p><p>2. It is simple and fervent.
 </p><p>3. It is reverent yet bold.
 </p><p>III. As to the spirit of it.
 </p><p>1. "Deal with thy servant"; a sense of obligation; a feeling of devotedness; a spirit of consecration to holy work.
 </p><p>2. "Deal.... according to thy mercy"; a sense of unworthiness; becoming humility; submissiveness to the divine will as to what form the mercy shall take; great faith in the mercy, its freeness and sufficiency.
 </p><p>3. "Teach me thy statutes." Longing for holiness, sense of ignorance, of weakness, of dependence upon special divine spiritual influence. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#125">Psa 119:125</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. An office accepted.
 </p><p>II. Fitness requested.
 </p><p>III. Discernment desired.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#125">Psa 119:125</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. A cheerful acknowledgment, "I am thy servant."
 </p><p>II. A desire implied - to serve more perfectly.
 </p><p>III. A need recognized - Divine instruction in holy service.
 </p><p>IV. A plea urged - "I am thy servant," therefore "Teach me," etc. - W. H. J. P.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#126">Psa 119:126-128</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. A terrible fact, "They have made void thy law": <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#126">Psa 119:126</a>.
 </p><p>II. Two blessed inferences, "Therefore, Therefore," etc.: <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#127">Psa 119:127, 19 119:128</a>.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#126">Psa 119:126</a>. - They make void the law, by denying inspiration, by exalting tradition, by antinomianism, by scepticism, by indifference, etc.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#126">Psa 119:126</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. There are times when sin is specially active and dominant.
 </p><p>II. Such times reveal the dependence of the church upon God.
 </p><p>III. Such times awaken the desires of the church for the intervention of God.
 </p><p>IV. Such times are the times when God does arise to plead his own cause. - W. H. J. P.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#126">Psa 119:126</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The work anticipated - the vindication of the divine law.
 </p><p>II. The work delayed.
 </p><p>III. The work executed, "It is time," etc. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#127">Psa 119:127</a>. - The world's assault upon the truth a reason for our loving it.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#127">Psa 119:127</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The object of love, "Thy commandments."
 </p><p>II. The degree of love, "above gold," etc.
 </p><p>III. The reason of this love - "therefore," etc., because its object must ultimately prevail. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#127">Psa 119:127</a>. - God's will versus the golden idol.
 </p><p>I. God's commandments are better than gold.
 </p><p>II. The love of them is proportionably nobler.
 </p><p>III. The unmeasurable superiority of character they produce. - W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#128">Psa 119:128</a> (first clause). - This view should be taken of all divine precepts in their bearing,
 </p><p>I. Christ-ward.
 </p><p>II. Self-ward.
 </p><p>III. World-ward.
 </p><p>IV. Church-ward.
 </p><p>V. Heaven-ward. - IV. J.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#128">Psa 119:128</a>. - The Bible right.
 </p><p>I. Its science is correct.
 </p><p>II. Its history is true.
 </p><p>III. Its promises are genuine.
 </p><p>IV. Its morality is perfect.
 </p><p>V. Its doctrines are divine. - W. W.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#128">Psa 119:128</a>. - Learn four lessons, - 
 </p><p>I. It is a good thing when wicked men do not praise the truth they cannot love.
 </p><p>II. It is a suspicious circumstance when they are found speaking well of any part of it; it is a Judas' kiss in order to betray its interests.
 </p><p>III. It must be right to accept and love what the wicked oppose.
 </p><p>IV. It is always safe to be on the opposite side to them. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#129">Psa 119:129-136</a>. - In this division the Psalmist - 
 </p><p>I. Praises God's word.
 </p><p>II. Shows his affection to it.
 </p><p>III. Prays for grace to keep it.
 </p><p>IV. Mourns for those who do not. - Adam Clarke.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#129">Psa 119:129</a>. - The wonderful character of the word a reason for obedience. So wonderfully pure, just, balanced, elevating. So much for our own benefit, for the good of society, and for the divine glory.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#129">Psa 119:129</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. What is wonderful in God's word should be believed.
 </p><p>II. What is believed should be obeyed. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#129">Psa 119:129</a>. - "Thy testimonies are wonderful."
 </p><p>I. The facts which they record are wonderful - so wonderful, that if the book recording them were now published for the first time, there would be no bounds to the avidity and curiosity with which it would be sought and perused.
 </p><p>II. The morality which they inculcate is wonderful.
 </p><p>III. If you turn from the morality to the doctrines of the Bible, your admiration will rather increase than diminish at the contents of the singular book.
 </p><p>IV. These testimonies are wonderful for the style in which they are written.
 </p><p>V. They are wonderful for their preservation in the world.
 </p><p>VI. They are wonderful for the effects which they have produced. - Hugh Hughes, 1838.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#129">Psa 119:129</a>. - "Thy testimonies are wonderful."
 </p><p>I. The ceremonial law is wonderful, because the mystery of our redemption-by the blood of Christ is pointed out in it.
 </p><p>II. The prophecies are wonderful, as predicting things, humanly speaking, so uncertain, and at such great distance of time, with so much accuracy.
 </p><p>III. The decalogue is wonderful, as containing in a very few words all the principles of justice and charity.
 </p><p>IV. Were we to go to the New Testament, here wonders rise on wonders! All is astonishing; but the Psalmist could not have had this in view. - Adam Clarke.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#129">Psa 119:129</a> (first clause). - 
 </p><p>I. Let us look at five of the wonders of the Bible.
 </p><p>1. Its authority. It prefaces every statement with a "Thus saith the Lord."
 </p><p>2. Its light.
 </p><p>3. Its power - it has a convincing, awakening, drawing, life-giving power.
 </p><p>4. Its depth.
 </p><p>5. Its universal adaptation.
 </p><p>II. Indicate three practical uses.
 </p><p>1. Study the Bible daily.
 </p><p>2. Pray for the Spirit to grave it on your heart with a pen of iron.
 </p><p>3. Practise it daily. - D. Macgregor.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#129">Psa 119:129</a>. - To whom and in what respects are God's testimonies wonderful?
 </p><p>I. To whom? To those, and those only, who through grace do know, believe, and experience the truth and power of them for themselves.
 </p><p>II. In what respects wonderful, i.e., astonishingly pleasing, delightful, and profitable (see <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#174">Psa 119:174</a>).
 </p><p>1. In respect of the Author and origin of them, whose they are and from whence they come.
 </p><p>2. In respect of the subject matter of them, which they contain and reveal.
 </p><p>3. In respect of the manner of language in which they are revealed and declared.
 </p><p>4. In respect of the multitude and variety of them suited to every ease.
 </p><p>5. In respect of the usefulness of them, and the great benefit and advantage he received from them.
 </p><p>6. In the respect of the pleasure and delight he finds in them (see <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#111">Psa 119:111</a>).
 </p><p>7. In respect of the final design, intent, and end of them, viz., eternal life, salvation, and glory. - Samuel Medley, 1738-1799.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#130">Psa 119:130</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The essential light of the word.
 </p><p>II. The dawn of it in the soul.
 </p><p>III. The great benefit of its advancing day.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#130">Psa 119:130</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The source of divine light to man, "Thy words."
 </p><p>II. Its force. It forces an entrance into the heart.
 </p><p>III. Its direction: "unto the simple."
 </p><p>IV. Its effect: "it giveth understanding." - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#130">Psa 119:130</a>. - A Bible Society Sermon.
 </p><p>I. Evidence from history and from personal experience that God's word has imparted the light of civilization, liberty, holiness.
 </p><p>II. Argument drawn from hence for the further spread of the word of God. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#130">Psa 119:130</a>. - The Self-evidencing Virtue of God's Word.
 </p><p>I. Prove it. "The entrance of thy word giveth light." If this be true, God's word is light; for only light can give light. But light is self-evidencing; it needs nothing to show its presence and its value but itself; so the word of God, to show its own truth and divinity to the believer.
 </p><p>1. His conscience proves it; in its conviction of sin; in its peace through the atoning blood.
 </p><p>2. His heart proves it; in its outgoings of love to the God, the Christ, and the righteousness revealed.
 </p><p>3. His experience In affliction and temptation proves it; in the solace and in the strength given by the word.
 </p><p>II. Answer an objection. "If God's word were self-evidencing as light is, then everyone would acknowledge it to be truth." Answer, No; for the law holds good in universal experience, that the "entrance" only of light gives light. Light cannot enter a blind man.
 </p><p>1. The Scriptures teach that men by nature are blind.
 </p><p>2. If all men did perceive, by merely reading and hearing the word, that it was light and truth, paradoxical as it may seem, the word would not be truth.
 </p><p>3. Hence the want of universal acknowledgment is not an objection, but a confirmation.
 </p><p>III. Show its importance.
 </p><p>1. It makes the believer independent of church authority for his faith.
 </p><p>2. He need not trouble to examine books of evidence; his faith is valid enough without them.
 </p><p>3. He who receives the word into his soul shall be satisfied of its truth and value. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#131">Psa 119:131</a>. - Panting for holiness. A rare hunger; the evidence of much grace, and the pledge of glory.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#132">Psa 119:132</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Look.
 </p><p>II. Love.
 </p><p>III. Use and wont.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#132">Psa 119:132</a>. - Fellowship with the righteous.
 </p><p>I. There are some who love God's name.
 </p><p>II. His mercy is the source of all the goodness they experience.
 </p><p>III. The Lord has been always accustomed to deal mercifully with them.
 </p><p>IV. His mercy towards them should encourage us to implore mercy for ourselves.
 </p><p>V. We should be anxious to secure the mercy that is peculiar to them.
 </p><p>VI. We should be content if God deals with us as he has always dealt with his people. - W. Jay.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#132">Psa 119:132</a>. - Divine use and wont.
 </p><p>I. God is accustomed to look upon and be merciful toward his people.
 </p><p>II. We are stirred up to specially desire such merciful dealings in time of affliction.
 </p><p>III. Love to God qualifies us for these loving looks and merciful dealings. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#132">Psa 119:132</a>. - Notice, - 
 </p><p>I. The mark of true believers: "Those that love thy name."
 </p><p>II. God's custom of dealing with them: "Be merciful as thou usest to do."
 </p><p>III. Their individual and earnest solicitude: "Look thou upon me." - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#133">Psa 119:133</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. A holy life is no work of chance, it is a masterpiece of order - the order of conformity to the prescribed rule; there is arithmetical and geometrical order: the proportional order; the order of relation; an order of period: holiness, as to the order, is seasonable, suitable.
 </p><p>II. The rule of this order - "in thy word."
 </p><p>III. The director chosen. See "Spurgeon's Sermons," No. 878: "A Well-ordered Life."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#133">Psa 119:133</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Order in outward life desired.
 </p><p>II. Order according to the divine idea.
 </p><p>III. Order in the government within.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#133">Psa 119:133</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Help needed.
 </p><p>1. To avoid sin.
 </p><p>2. To be holy.
 </p><p>II. Help sought.
 </p><p>1. From below: "thy word."
 </p><p>2. From above: "order," etc., and "let not," etc. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#133">Psa 119:133</a>. - Sin's sway in the soul.
 </p><p>I. Fervently deprecated.
 </p><p>1. Realization of the horrors of its rule.
 </p><p>2. Recognition of the better power.
 </p><p>3. Thorough exclusion sought.
 </p><p>II. Wisely combated.
 </p><p>1. Practicalness as well as prayerfulness.
 </p><p>2. Regard had to little "steps."
 </p><p>3. Steps to be governed by divine rule.
 </p><p>4. System not trusted apart from God. - W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#133">Psa 119:133</a>. - Notice, - 
 </p><p>I. The right path for human feet: "In thy word."
 </p><p>II. The needed help to control the steps: "Order my steps."
 </p><p>III. The perverting power of a dominant sin: "Let not any," etc. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#134">Psa 119:134</a>. - What sins may be produced by oppression. What obedience ought to come from those who are set free.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#134">Psa 119:134</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The course to be pursued, "thy precepts."
 </p><p>II. The opposition to that course: "the oppression of men."
 </p><p>1. Human opinions.
 </p><p>2. Human examples.
 </p><p>3. Human sympathies.
 </p><p>4. Interests.
 </p><p>5. Persecutions.
 </p><p>III. The resistance to that opposition, "Deliver me, so will I," etc. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#134">Psa 119:134</a>. - Hindrances removed.
 </p><p>I. The impeding influence of persecution.
 </p><p>II. The prayer of the persecuted one.
 </p><p>III. The conduct of the delivered one (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/luk001.htm#074">Luk 1:74</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/luk001.htm#075">Luk 1:75</a>). - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#134">Psa 119:134</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. How some men oppress their fellows. By the laws they make - as statesmen. By the books they write - as authors. By the tyranny they exercise - as masters. By the lives they live - as professors. By the sermons they deliver - as ministers!
 </p><p>II. How the prayer of the oppressed may be answered. By the gift of wise and good statesmen. By increase of sound literature. By the conversion or removal of hard masters. By a baptism of the Spirit on the church. - W. W.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#135">Psa 119:135</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. A choice position: "thy servant."
 </p><p>II. A choice delight: "thy face to shine."
 </p><p>III. A choice privilege: "teach me thy statutes."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#135">Psa 119:135</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. God in the word: "Thy word."
 </p><p>II. God for the word: "Teach me," etc.
 </p><p>III. God with the word: "Make thy face," etc. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#135">Psa 119:135</a>. - Sunshine.
 </p><p>I. The light in which we can best learn our lessons - God's favour shown in pardon, justification, adoption, assurance, etc.
 </p><p>II. The lessons we should learn in the light - grace is productive of holiness. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#135">Psa 119:135</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. A rich historic promise (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/num006.htm#025">Num 6:25</a>). Its sublime origin and associations.
 </p><p>II. The new prayer born of it.
 </p><p>1. Looks up for the face Divine; the same in its majestic sweetness that has watched generations decay since the word was first spoken.
 </p><p>2. Asks to know its shinings. Light of fatherhood, etc.
 </p><p>III. The old prayer repeated: "Teach me thy statutes." Last time in the Psalm.
 </p><p>1. Our need of teaching - oft-repeated prayer.
 </p><p>2. The intimate connection between obedience and the shinings of God's face. - W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#136">Psa 119:136</a>. - Abundant sorrow for abounding sin. Other men's sins the saint's own sorrows. He thinks of the good God provoked, of the sinners themselves debased, of their death, and their perdition.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#136">Psa 119:136</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Occasion of his grief: "they keep not thy law."
 </p><p>II. Extent of his grief: "rivers," etc. See examples in Jeremiah, Ezra, Paul, Christ himself.
 </p><p>III. Effect of his grief. To warn, teach, invite, and exhort them - as in his Psalms. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#136">Psa 119:136</a>. - Sacred tears.
 </p><p>I. The world sinning.
 </p><p>II. The church weeping.
 </p><p>III. It is time the world began to weep for itself. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#136">Psa 119:136</a>. - I weep, because,
 </p><p>I. Of the dishonour done to the Law-giver.
 </p><p>II. Of the injury done to the law-breaker.
 </p><p>III. Of the wrong done to the law-abiding.
