"That kingly prophet, that wept so plentifully for his own offences (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa006.htm#006">Psa 6:6</a>), had yet floods of tears left to bewail his people's" (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#136">Psa 119:136</a> ). - Thomas Adams. "Bendetti, a Franciscan monk, author of the Stabat Mater, one day was found weeping, and when asked the reason of his tears, he exclaimed, 'I weep because Love goes about unloved.'" - W. H. J. P.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#137">Psa 119:137</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#138">Psa 119:138</a>. - Solemn contemplation.
 </p><p>I. The contemplation of the deep and awful display of the divine character is good for the soul.
 </p><p>II. It will lead to a conviction of the righteousness of God's character and administration.
 </p><p>III. It will result in loyal submission. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#137">Psa 119:137</a>. - A consideration of divine righteousness. Convinces us of sin, reconciles us to trying providences, excites a desire to imitate, arouses to reverent adoration.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#137">Psa 119:137</a>. - God is righteous.
 </p><p>I. In his commands.
 </p><p>II. In his threatenings.
 </p><p>III. In his chastisements.
 </p><p>IV. In his judgments.
 </p><p>V. In his promises. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#138">Psa 119:138</a>. - "Very faithful." Based on a faithful covenant; confirmed by faithful promises; carried out by a faithful Redeemer; enjoyed hitherto; relied on for the future. "Though we believe not, yet he abideth faithful."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#139">Psa 119:139</a>. - "Zeal."
 </p><p>I. Consuming self.
 </p><p>II. Inflamed by that which would naturally quench it.
 </p><p>III. Fed upon God's words.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#139">Psa 119:139</a>. - "Zeal."
 </p><p>I. Flourishing in an uncompromising atmosphere.
 </p><p>II. Attaining an astonishing growth.
 </p><p>III. Accomplishing a blessed work - the consumption of self. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#139">Psa 119:139</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The object of his zeal - "Thy words."
 </p><p>II. The occasion of his zeal: "Mine enemies," etc.
 </p><p>III. The fervour of his zeal: "My zeal hath consumed me." - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#140">Psa 119:140</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. An awakened sinner adoring the holy law.
 </p><p>II. A saint loving it because the pure love the pure.
 </p><p>III. A saint among sinners loving the law all the more for its contrast.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#140">Psa 119:140</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The crystal stream.
 </p><p>1. Flows from under the throne.
 </p><p>2. Mirrors heaven.
 </p><p>3. Undefiled through the ages.
 </p><p>4. Nourishes holiness as it flows.
 </p><p>II. The enraptured pilgrim.
 </p><p>1. Keeping by its brink.
 </p><p>2. Delighted with its lucid depths.
 </p><p>3. Pleased with its mirrored revelations - self, heaven, God.
 </p><p>4. Cleansed and refreshed by its waters. - W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#140">Psa 119:140</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The purity of God's word.
 </p><p>1. It proceeds from a perfectly pure source: "Thy word."
 </p><p>2. It reveals a purity otherwise unknown.
 </p><p>3. It treats impure subjects with absolute purity.
 </p><p>4. It inculcates the most perfect purity.
 </p><p>5. It produces such purity in those who are subject to its power.
 </p><p>II. The love which its purity inspires in gracious souls.
 </p><p>1. They love it because, while it reveals their natural impurity, it shows them how to escape from it.
 </p><p>2. They love it because it conforms them to its own purity.
 </p><p>3. They love it because to a pure heart the purity of the word is one of its chief commendations.
 </p><p>III. The evidences of this love to the pure word.
 </p><p>1. Desire to possess it in its purity.
 </p><p>2. Subjection to its spirit and teachings.
 </p><p>3. Zeal for its honour and diffusion. - W. H. J. P.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#141">Psa 119:141-144</a>. - A mournful song and a joyful refrain. Stanza I. "I am small and despised." Refrain. The everlasting righteousness of God. Stanza II. "Trouble and anguish have seized me." Refrain. The everlasting righteousness of God. - C. A. D,
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#141">Psa 119:141</a>. - Here is - 
 </p><p>I. David pious, and yet poor. He was a man after God's own heart, and yet "small and despised" in his own account and in account of many others.
 </p><p>II. David poor and yet pious; "small and despised" for his strict and serious godliness; yet his conscience can witness for him, that he "did not forget God's precepts." - M. Henry.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#141">Psa 119:141</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The source of man's littleness is in himself.
 </p><p>II. The source of his greatness is in the Divine word. Hence the greatest philosopher is a small man compared with the most uneducated whose delight is in the law of God, and who meditates, etc. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#141">Psa 119:141</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. A little scholar.
 </p><p>II. A quick learner.
 </p><p>III. A firm remembrancer.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#141">Psa 119:141</a>. - "Unknown, yet well known."
 </p><p>I. The estimate formed of the believer by the world.
 </p><p>II. The estimate formed of the believer by himself.
 </p><p>III. The profession made by the believer to God.
 </p><p>IV. On a review, a revised estimate of the believer, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/co1001.htm#027">Co1 1:27</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/jam002.htm#005">Jam 2:5</a> in C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#142">Psa 119:142</a>. - Righteousness, immutability, and truth combined in the revelation of God.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#143">Psa 119:143</a>. - Mingled emotions.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#143">Psa 119:143</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The dark cloud. Trouble, etc.
 </p><p>II. His silver lining. Yet, etc.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#143">Psa 119:143</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The saint cast into prison.
 </p><p>1. The jailers, "Trouble and anguish."
 </p><p>2. Their proceeding, "take hold" and make him fast.
 </p><p>II. Songs in the night.
 </p><p>1. Blessed theme, "thy commandments."
 </p><p>2. Ecstatic melodies: "delights."
 </p><p>III. Let the prisoners hear them.
 </p><p>1. Pain-held, sin-held, despair held.
 </p><p>2. It is matter and melody to open prisons. - W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#143">Psa 119:143</a>. - Consider, - 
 </p><p>I. The excellency of the word, in that it gives delight when trouble and anguish oppress.
 </p><p>II. The great kindness of God in so framing his word that it can give delight at such a time, and under such circumstances.
 </p><p>III. The disposition of the believer to resort to the word for delight, when others give themselves over to vain grief and despondency.
 </p><p>IV. The blessed position of the believer, in that he need never be without joy. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#144">Psa 119:144</a>. - Everlasting righteousness revealed in the word, and producing everlasting life in believers.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#144">Psa 119:144</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Eternal truths.
 </p><p>II. Eternal life dependent upon them.
 </p><p>III. A cry from amid these everlasting hills. - W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#144">Psa 119:144</a> (last clause). - 
 </p><p>I. Consider the prayer in its simplicity.
 </p><p>1. It is suitable for the awakened sinner.
 </p><p>2. For the Christian struggling against temptation.
 </p><p>3. For the suffering believer.
 </p><p>4. For the worker.
 </p><p>5. For aspiring minds in the church of God.
 </p><p>6. For expiring saints.
 </p><p>II. The prayer more fully opened up.
 </p><p>1. Here is want confessed.
 </p><p>2. The prayer is evidently put upon the footing of free grace - "Give."
 </p><p>III. Lay bare the argument in the prayer.
 </p><p>1. The word of God, when practically and experimentally understood, is a pledge of life.
 </p><p>2. The word of God is the incorruptible "seed" which liveth and abideth for ever.
 </p><p>3. It is the food of life.
 </p><p>4. It is the very flower and crown and glory of true life.
 </p><p>5. It is righteous.
 </p><p>6. It is everlasting. See "Spurgeon's Sermons," No. 1572, "Alive."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#145">Psa 119:145-148</a>. - The cry.
 </p><p>I. Whence it came, from my heart.
 </p><p>II. Whither it went, to the Lord.
 </p><p>III. When it was heard, at dawn and dark.
 </p><p>IV. What it sought, hearing, salvation.
 </p><p>V. What it promised, obedience.
 </p><p>VI. How it was sustained, by hope in God's word. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#145">Psa 119:145</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#146">Psa 119:146</a>. - The soul's cry.
 </p><p>I. The depth from which it rose.
 </p><p>II. The height it reached.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#145">Psa 119:145</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#146">Psa 119:146</a>. - Childlike prayer.
 </p><p>I. In its ring, "I cried."
 </p><p>II. In its directness: "to thee."
 </p><p>III. In its outburst "whole heart."
 </p><p>IV. In its outcries: "hear me"; "save me."
 </p><p>V. In its promise of better behaviour, "I will keep thy statutes." - W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#145">Psa 119:145</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The model of prayer, "I cried with my whole heart."
 </p><p>II. The object of prayer - "Hear me, O Lord."
 </p><p>III. The accompaniment of prayer, "I will keep thy statutes."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#146">Psa 119:146</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Prayer remembered.
 </p><p>II. Prayer continued. "Save me."
 </p><p>III. Prayer yielding fruit: "I shall keep," etc.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#146">Psa 119:146</a>. - Salvation.
 </p><p>I. A likely path to it - prayer, cry on.
 </p><p>II. The proper place for it, "unto thee"; not man, not the heart.
 </p><p>III. A sound view of it, "keep thy testimonies." Not to escape hell, or gain heaven, but to please and love God. - W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#147">Psa 119:147</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#148">Psa 119:148</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The heavenly companions' prayer and meditation. Inseparable. Mutually helpful.
 </p><p>II. Their favourite seasons' times of stillness; night; the hour before day.
 </p><p>III. Their volume and night-lamp, "Thy word;" "Hope."
 </p><p>Or - 
 </p><p>I. A grand plea: "Thy lovingkindness." Who can match it? Who can measure it? Who can mar it?
 </p><p>II. An insignificant pleader: "my voice." What can "my voice" ever say to keep step with "thy lovingkindness"? Asking too much out of the question.
 </p><p>III. A clever petition ("according to thy judgment"); requesting life; stolen from God's mouth. God's lovingkindness is matched by God's own promise. - W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#147">Psa 119:147</a>. - Observe in this David's diligence.
 </p><p>I. That it was a personal, closet, or secret prayer; "I cried"; I alone, with thee in secret.
 </p><p>II. That it was an early morning prayer: "I prevented the dawning of the morning."
 </p><p>III. That it was a vehement and earnest prayer; for it is expressed by crying. - T. Manton.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#147">Psa 119:147</a>. - Early rising commended.
 </p><p>I. A fit time for prayer.
 </p><p>II. For reading the word.
 </p><p>III. For indulging the emotions excited by it: "I hoped in thy word."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#148">Psa 119:148</a>. - "The Inexhaustibleness of the Bible." A sermon by Harry Melvill, at "The Golden Lecture." 1850.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#148">Psa 119:148</a>. - Meditation. Appropriate time, and fruitful subject.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#148">Psa 119:148</a>. - Meditation in the word well worth self-denial and care on the part of the Christian.
 </p><p>I. Without meditation reading is a waste of time and an indignity offered to the word.
 </p><p>II. Meditation with prayer, but not prayer without meditation, will discover the sense of the word, when all other means fail; and it has this advantage, that the meaning sinks into the mind.
 </p><p>III. Meditation extracts sweetness from the promises, and nourishment from the whole truth.
 </p><p>IV. Meditation makes a wise teacher and an efficient worker of one who has little natural skill or learning.
 </p><p>V. Meditation subjects the soul to the sanctifying power of the word.
 </p><p>VI. Meditation is an invitation to the Holy Spirit to bless the soul, for he is closely associated with the truth, and delights to see the truth honoured. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#149">Psa 119:149</a>. - Prayer - hearing the result of love; prayer - answering ruled by wisdom.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#149">Psa 119:149</a>. - Quickening.
 </p><p>I. A prayer of unquestionable necessity: "quicken me."
 </p><p>II. Twin pleas of irresistible power: "thy lovingkindness:" "thy judgment." - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#149">Psa 119:149</a>. - The two accordings.
 </p><p>I. The "according," to which a believer hopes to be heard by God, "Hear my voice according unto thy lovingkindness."
 </p><p>1. The believer is fully aware of his own unworthiness, and the imperfections of his prayers, therefore he would have God to accept him and interpret them after the rule of his own lovingkindness.
 </p><p>2. Nor does he hope in vain; God's lovingkindness overlooks the imperfections, and supplies the omissions.
 </p><p>3. What a blessed thing it is, that while the Holy Spirit helps our infirmities, the groanings that cannot be uttered are read in their true meaning by divine lovingkindness!
 </p><p>II. The "according" to which he expects to be answered by God: "Quicken me according to thy judgment." "Judgment" here may mean the revealed word. Then - 
 </p><p>1. He expects to be answered certainly.
 </p><p>2. He expects to be answered wisely.
 </p><p>3. He expects to be answered fully, as all his needs require.
 </p><p>4. He expects that every answer should quicken spiritual life, making him holy. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#150">Psa 119:150</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#151">Psa 119:151</a>. - Against mischief-makers.
 </p><p>I. They press as near as they can to harm us.
 </p><p>II. They get far from right to get more liberty to injure us.
 </p><p>III. The Lord is nearer than they.
 </p><p>IV. God's truth is our shield and sword.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#150">Psa 119:150</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#151">Psa 119:151</a>. - Foes near: the Friend nearer.
 </p><p>I. The believer viewing with alarm the approach of his foes: "They draw near."
 </p><p>II. The believer viewing with comfort the presence of his friend - "Thou art near-" <a href="../../../bib/kjv/gen015.htm#001">Gen 15:1</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/kg2006.htm#014">Kg2 6:14-17</a>. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#150">Psa 119:150</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#151">Psa 119:151</a>. - Two beleaguering hosts.
 </p><p>I. The host of evil: Near - 
 </p><p>1. Demons, godless men, spiritual foes of world and heart.
 </p><p>2. Mischief in their van.
 </p><p>3. Law and truth left far behind.
 </p><p>4. Seeking to narrow their lines.
 </p><p>5. Thus are all saints beset.
 </p><p>II. The host of God: nearer - Jehovah, his angels, and battalions of truths holy and immortal: "Thou and all thy commandments."
 </p><p>1. Entrenched in the reason: "are truth."
 </p><p>2. Camped in the heart's pavilion: "near."
 </p><p>3. Forming impregnable lines within those of the foe. - W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#150">Psa 119:150</a>. - Consider - 
 </p><p>I. Whether the description here given does not apply, more or less, to all believers in Christ: "They that follow after mischief."
 </p><p>1. Some men undoubtedly and of set purpose do follow after mischief; they make themselves the tempters of others, and delight in it.
 </p><p>2. Others, who do not delight in it, yet cannot help the mischievous effect of their example.
 </p><p>3. The very morality of many unbelievers enables them to carry the pernicious influence of their unbelief where the immorally wicked cannot come.
 </p><p>4. Even regular attendants at public worship may by their indecision encourage others in delay.
 </p><p>II. The dangerous position of all to whom the description, in any measure, belongs, "They are far from thy law."
 </p><p>1. They are so, in that they are unbelievers; for "this is his commandment, that we shall believe," etc.
 </p><p>2. They are so, in that they are a cause of evil to others; for we are commanded to love and do good.
 </p><p>3. To be far from God's law is to be nigh unto God's righteous wrath.
 </p><p>4. For the sake of others, as well as their own, men should believe in Christ, and through faith become sanctified. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#151">Psa 119:151</a> (last clause). - The commandments of the Lord are true in principle; they lead to true living, if carried out; they truly reward the obedient; they never lead to falsehood, nor cause to be deluded.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#152">Psa 119:152</a>. - Knowledge of the word.
 </p><p>I. It is well to know it as God's own word.
 </p><p>II. As founded in truth.
 </p><p>III. As founded for ever.
 </p><p>IV. The earlier we know this the better.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#153">Psa 119:153-159</a>. - The two considers. The subjects, the prayers, the arguments.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#153">Psa 119:153</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#154">Psa 119:154</a> - Here - 
 </p><p>I. David prays for succour in distress. "Is any afflicted? let him pray"; let him pray as David doth here.
 </p><p>1. He hath an eye to God's pity and prays, "Consider mine affliction"; take it unto thy thoughts, and all the circumstances, and sit not by as one unconcerned. God is never unmindful of his people's afflictions, but he will have us to "put him in remembrance" (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/isa043.htm#026">Isa 43:26</a>), to spread our case before him, and then leave it to his compassionate consideration to do in it as in his wisdom he shall think fit, in his own time and way.
 </p><p>2. He has an eye to God's power, and prays, "Deliver me," and again, "Deliver me." Consider my troubles and bring me out of them. God has promised deliverance (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa001.htm#001">Psa 1:1-5</a>), and we may pray for it with submission to his will, and with regard to his glory, that we may serve him the better.
 </p><p>3. He has an eye to God's righteousness, and prays, "Plead my cause" be thou my patron and advocate, and take me for thy client. David had a just cause, but his adversaries were many and mighty, and he was in danger of being run down by them - he therefore begs of God to clear his integrity, and silence their false accusations. If God do not plead his people's cause, who will? He is righteous, and they commit themselves to him, and therefore he will do it, and do it effectually, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/isa051.htm#022">Isa 51:22</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/jer050.htm#034">Jer 50:34</a>.
 </p><p>4. He has an eye to God's grace, and prays, "Quicken me" Lord, I am weak, and unable to bear my troubles; my spirit is apt to droop and sink, Oh, that thou wouldst revive and comfort me, till the deliverance is wrought
 </p><p>III. He pleads his dependence upon the word of God, and his devotedness to his conduct. "Quicken" and "deliver me according to thy word" of promise; "for I do not forget thy precepts." The closer we cleave to the word of God, both as our rule and as our stay, the more assurance we may have of deliverance in due time. - M. Henry.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#153">Psa 119:153</a>. - The sick man's prayer.
 </p><p>I. The medicine remembered.
 </p><p>II. The physician sent for.
 </p><p>III. The physician considering the case.
 </p><p>IV. The healing wrought. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#153">Psa 119:153</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Lord, do not forget my sorrow.
 </p><p>II. I do not forget thy law. <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#154">Psa 119:154</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#156">Psa 119:156</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#159">Psa 119:159</a>. - The threefold quickening. A capital subject, if the contexts are carefully considered.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#154">Psa 119:154</a>. - Intercession, deliverance, quickening, and all in faithfulness to the word.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#154">Psa 119:154</a>. - A prayer.
 </p><p>I. For promised defence.
 </p><p>II. For promised deliverance.
 </p><p>III. For promised revival. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#154">Psa 119:154</a>. - The advocate.
 </p><p>I. The soul hard-pressed by the accuser - in the conscience (l <a href="../../../bib/kjv/joh003.htm#020">Joh 3:20</a>); before the world; at the throne of grace (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/zac003.htm#001">Zac 3:1-10</a>); at the bar of judgment.
 </p><p>II. The accused soul committing its case to the Advocate, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/jo1002.htm#002">Jo1 2:2</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/ti2001.htm#012">Ti2 1:12</a>.
 </p><p>III. How the case will go. He never lost one yet. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#155">Psa 119:155</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. An awful distance.
 </p><p>II. A distance never decreased by seeking.
 </p><p>III. A distance increased by sinning.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#155">Psa 119:155</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. When salvation is far off.
 </p><p>II. When it is near.
 </p><p>Or - 
 </p><p>I. When the word is far off salvation is far off.
 </p><p>II. When the word is near salvation is near. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#155">Psa 119:155</a>. - How to avoid salvation.
 </p><p>I. Salvation is inseparable from conformity to God's law: <a href="../../../bib/kjv/lev018.htm#005">Lev 18:5</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/luk010.htm#025">Luk 10:25-28</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/mat019.htm#017">Mat 19:17</a>.
 </p><p>II. Salvation is brought to law-breakers by the Law-giver condescending to become the Law-keeper and the Law-victim. Salvation is avoided by those who refuse to be conformed to the eternal law Or will of God. They perish themselves: their own sin punishes them, necessity punishes them. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#155">Psa 119:155</a>. - A syllogism on salvation.
 </p><p>I. Salvation and obedience go together.
 </p><p>1. Have a common centre - God, his arm and his lips.
 </p><p>2. A mutual relation: we are saved in order to obedience. In obeying we are being saved. Without obedience there is no salvation.
 </p><p>3. An identical aim - our good and God's glory.
 </p><p>4. Obedience and salvation are inseparable for ever.
 </p><p>II. The godless are far from obedience.
 </p><p>1. Commands avoided.
 </p><p>2. Submission excluded.
 </p><p>III. Therefore they are far from salvation. They will not have the one; they cannot have the other. - W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#156">Psa 119:156</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. A great need.
 </p><p>II. Laid before a great Lord.
 </p><p>III. Great favours pleaded.
 </p><p>IV. A great mercy sought: "Quicken me."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#156">Psa 119:156</a>. - Just, and the Quickener.
 </p><p>I. Spiritual life is the gift of God's mercy.
 </p><p>II. Its continuance depends on the exercise of God's power.
 </p><p>III. We may therefore plead for quickening on the ground of God's justice. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#156">Psa 119:156</a>. - The saint,
 </p><p>I. Lost in admiration.
 </p><p>1. Of God's tender mercies.
 </p><p>2. He cries out at their greatness. They are numerous. Greatly tender. Great and tender; (exquisite combination!).
 </p><p>II. Filled with animation. The child of his admiration.
 </p><p>1. The arrow-like prayer, "Quicken me." To be like, to be true to, such a God.
 </p><p>2. The bow in the hand, "according to thy judgments." - W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#156">Psa 119:156</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The tenderness of God's greatness.
 </p><p>II. The greatness of God's tenderness.
 </p><p>III. The Stimulus to life found in his great and tender presence.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#157">Psa 119:157</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. A word of multitude: "many."
 </p><p>II. A tendency of dread, viz., a tendency to decline.
 </p><p>III. A note of consolation, "yet do I' not decline."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#158">Psa 119:158</a>. - A grievous sight.
 </p><p>I. Transgressors beyond God's bounds.
 </p><p>II. Bounds so kindly set: "thy word."
 </p><p>III. Transgressions so wantonly ungrateful, so terribly dangerous, so fatal.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#158">Psa 119:158</a>. - Sorrow over sinners.
 </p><p>I. A sight we cannot avoid seeing.
 </p><p>II. A sorrow we ought not to avoid feeling. (See Lot: <a href="../../../bib/kjv/pe2002.htm#007">Pe2 2:7</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/pe2002.htm#008">Pe2 2:8</a>. Moses: <a href="../../../bib/kjv/deu009.htm#018">Deu 9:18</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/deu009.htm#019">Deu 9:19</a>. Samuel: <a href="../../../bib/kjv/sa1015.htm#011">Sa1 15:11</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/jer009.htm#001">Jer 9:1</a>. Paul: <a href="../../../bib/kjv/phi003.htm#018">Phi 3:18</a>. Christ: <a href="../../../bib/kjv/luk019.htm#041">Luk 19:41</a>.)
 </p><p>III. A reason we will not avoid endorsing.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#158">Psa 119:158</a>. - A righteous man cannot but be grieved at the sins of the wicked. He sees in them, - 
 </p><p>I. The violation of the divine law which he loves.
 </p><p>II. Ungrateful rebellion against the God he worships.
 </p><p>III. Contempt for the gospel of salvation and the blood of Christ.
 </p><p>IV. The dominion of Satan, the enemy of his God.
 </p><p>V. The degradation of souls which might have been sacred temples.
 </p><p>VI. Prophetic signs of an awful, everlasting retribution. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#159">Psa 119:159</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. His Own love avowed.
 </p><p>II. God's love pleaded.
 </p><p>III. Renewed life implored.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#159">Psa 119:159</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Attention invited, "Consider how."
 </p><p>II. Profession made, "I love thy precepts."
 </p><p>III. Petition offered: "Quicken me," etc.
 </p><p>IV. Plea suggested: "according to," etc. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#159">Psa 119:159</a>. - My love and thy lovingkindness. The saint's love.
 </p><p>I. Avowed. "Thou knowest all things," etc.
 </p><p>II. Submitted. In humble insistance on its sincerity. In sense of its insufficiency. In prayer to God not to overlook it.
 </p><p>III. Lost sight of in the sudden glory of God's lovingkindness. Where is my love now?
 </p><p>IV. Recovered and humbly brought for quickening. Lord, I'll say no more about it: "Quicken me." - W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#159">Psa 119:159</a>. - Quicken me for love's sake.
 </p><p>I. A prayer for quickened life.
 </p><p>II. Awakened by love to the divine rule of life.
 </p><p>III. Enforced by the plea of that love.
 </p><p>IV. Addressed to the God of love. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#159">Psa 119:159</a>. - Consider, - 
 </p><p>I. The holy unsatisfiedness of the believer: "Quicken me" etc.
 </p><p>1. A prayer frequently occurring in the Psalm, and always urged with great earnestness.
 </p><p>2. Its importunity proves the possession of spiritual life; in fact, none but the living ones crave quickening.
 </p><p>3. The most earnest feel the most acutely their indwelling sin, and appreciate most highly thorough sanctification.
 </p><p>4. Thus, this is, perhaps, the only unsatisfiedness perfectly pure in its character.
 </p><p>II. The assuring Divine attribute to which he can appeal, "According to thy lovingkindness."
 </p><p>1. An attribute, not only made known in the word, but made manifest to us in our experience of its gentle dealing.
 </p><p>2. An attribute that covers sin, and is touched with a feeling of our infirmities.
 </p><p>3. An attribute that must be affected with the cry for quickening grace.
 </p><p>III. The consideration he ought to be able to lay before God: "Consider how I love thy precepts."
 </p><p>1. Because from the word he learnt of the lovingkindness, and through it received life.
 </p><p>2. Without it the prayer cannot be genuine.
 </p><p>3. It is a good reason for expecting more grace; for "whosoever hath, to him shall be given," etc. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#160">Psa 119:160</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Early: "true from the beginning."
 </p><p>II. Late: "endureth for ever."
 </p><p>Or, Truth and immutability the believer's Jachin and Boaz.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#161">Psa 119:161</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#162">Psa 119:162</a>. - God's word, the object of godly fear and godly joy.
 </p><p>I. It makes the heart quake by its purity and power.
 </p><p>II. It makes the heart rejoice by its grace and truth. - W. H. J. P.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#161">Psa 119:161</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Wrong without cause.
 </p><p>II. Right with abundant cause.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#161">Psa 119:161</a> (second clause). - Awe of God's word - its propriety, its hallowed influence, the evil of its absence.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#161">Psa 119:161</a>. - Restrained by awe.
 </p><p>I. The causelessness of persecution.
 </p><p>II. The temptations to evil occasioned thereby - to revenge: to apostasy.
 </p><p>III. The safeguard against falling: awe of God's word. <a href="../../../bib/kjv/sa1024.htm#006">Sa1 24:6</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/dan003.htm#016">Dan 3:16-18</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/act004.htm#019">Act 4:19</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/act005.htm#029">Act 5:29</a>. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#162">Psa 119:162</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The treasure hid, "great spoil" hidden in the divine word.
 </p><p>II. The treasure found: "as one that findeth," etc.
 </p><p>1. By reading.
 </p><p>2. By meditation.
 </p><p>3. By prayer.
 </p><p>III. The treasure enjoyed, "I rejoice," etc. - G. R,
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#162">Psa 119:162</a>. - David's joy over God's word he compares to the joy of the warrior when he finds great spoil.
 </p><p>I. This great joy is sometimes aroused by the fact that there is a word of God.
 </p><p>1. The Scriptures are a revealing of God.
 </p><p>2. The guide of our life.
 </p><p>3. A sure pledge of mercy.
 </p><p>4. The beginning of communion with God.
 </p><p>5. The instrument of usefulness.
 </p><p>II. Frequently the joy of the believer in the word arises out of his having had to battle to obtain a grasp of it.
 </p><p>1. We have had to fight over certain doctrines before we could really come at them.
 </p><p>2. The same may be said of the promises.
 </p><p>3. Of the precepts.
 </p><p>4. Of the threatenings.
 </p><p>5. Even about the word which reveals Christ.
 </p><p>III. At times the joy of the believer lies in enjoying God's word without any fighting at all - "One that findeth."
 </p><p>IV. There is a joy arising out of the very fact that Holy Scripture may be considered to be a spoil.
 </p><p>1. A spoil is the end of uncertainty.
 </p><p>2. It is the weakening of the adversary for any future attacks.
 </p><p>3. It gives a sense of victory.
 </p><p>4. There is, in dividing the spoil, profit, pleasure, and honour.
 </p><p>5. The spoiling of the enemy is a prophecy of rest. See "Spurgeon's Sermons," No. 1641: "Great Spoil."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#163">Psa 119:163</a>. - Opposite poles of the Christian character.
 </p><p>I. Why I hate lying, because it comes from the devil (Pro. 8:44, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/act005.htm#003">Act 5:3</a>), it leads to the devil (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/rev021.htm#008">Rev 21:8</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/rev022.htm#015">Rev 22:15</a>): it is base, dangerous, degrading (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/pro019.htm#005">Pro 19:5</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/ti1004.htm#002">Ti1 4:2</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/ti2003.htm#013">Ti2 3:13</a>): it is hated by the Lord (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/pro006.htm#016">Pro 6:16</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/pro006.htm#017">Pro 6:17</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/pro012.htm#022">Pro 12:22</a>).
 </p><p>II. Why I love the law. Because it emanates from God; is the reflection of his character; is the ideal of my character.
 </p><p>III. How I came thus to hate and love. By the grace of God: <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#029">Psa 119:29</a>. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#163">Psa 119:163</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Opposite things.
 </p><p>II. Opposite feelings.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#164">Psa 119:164</a>. - Praise rendered. Frequently, statedly, heartily, intelligently.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#164">Psa 119:164</a>. - Perpetual praise.
 </p><p>I. True praise is ever warranted.
 </p><p>II. True praise is ever welcome.
 </p><p>III. True praise is never weary. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#164">Psa 119:164</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Some never praise thee; but, "seven times a day," etc.; for I delight to do so. "Thy righteous judgments" are a terror to them, a joy to me.
 </p><p>II. Some feebly and coldly praise thee, while, "seven times," etc. My warm devotion must frequently express itself in praise.
 </p><p>III. Some are content with occasionally praising thee, but, "seven times," etc. They think it enough to begin and end the day with praise, while all the day long I am in the spirit of praise.
 </p><p>IV. Some soon cease to praise thee, but, "seven times," etc. Not seven times only, but "unto seventy times seven." Even without ceasing, will I praise thee. - W. H. J. P.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#165">Psa 119:165</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Great love to a great law.
 </p><p>II. Great peace under great disquietude.
 </p><p>III. Great upholding from all stumbling blocks.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#165">Psa 119:165</a>. - Perfect peace.
 </p><p>I. The law of God should be regarded with love.
 </p><p>II. Love to the law is productive of great peace. Peace with God through the blood of reconciliation: peace with self by good conscience and suppression of evil desires: peace with men by charity.
 </p><p>III. The peace which springs from love to the law is a security against stumbling: "nothing shall offend them;" neither the daily cross (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/mar010.htm#021">Mar 10:21</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/mar010.htm#022">Mar 10:22</a>); nor the fiery trial (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/mar004.htm#007">Mar 4:7</a>); nor the humbling doctrine (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/joh006.htm#060">Joh 6:60</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/joh006.htm#066">Joh 6:66</a>, etc.). - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#165">Psa 119:165</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The characters described, "they which love thy law."
 </p><p>II. The blessing they enjoy, "great peace."
 </p><p>III. The evils they escape, "nothing shall offend them." - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#165">Psa 119:165</a>. - The peace and security of the godly.
 </p><p>I. Their peace. It arises from - 
 </p><p>1. Freedom from an accusing conscience.
 </p><p>2. Conformity to the requirements of the law.
 </p><p>3. Enjoyment of the privileges revealed in the law.
 </p><p>4. Assurance of divine approval and benediction.
 </p><p>II. Their security.
 </p><p>1. They are prepared for every duty.
 </p><p>2. They are proof against every temptation.
 </p><p>3. They are pledged to final perseverance.
 </p><p>4. They have the promise of divine protection. - W. H. J. P.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#165">Psa 119:165</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. An honourable title, "They which love thy law."
 </p><p>II. A good possession, "Great peace have they."
 </p><p>III. A blessed immunity, "Nothing shall offend them." - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#166">Psa 119:166</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. A hope which is not ashamed.
 </p><p>II. A life which is not ashamed.
 </p><p>III. A God of whom he is not ashamed.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#166">Psa 119:166</a>. - A good hope through grace.
 </p><p>I. Salvation is God's gift, "thy salvation."
 </p><p>II. Is apprehended by hope, "I have hoped."
 </p><p>III. Is accompanied by obedience, "and done thy commandments." <a href="../../../bib/kjv/heb006.htm#009">Heb 6:9</a>. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#167">Psa 119:167</a>. - Past and present.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#167">Psa 119:167</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The more we keep God's testimonies the more we shall love them.
 </p><p>II. The more we love them the more we shall keep them. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#167">Psa 119:167</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The jewels, "Thy testimonies."
 </p><p>1. Rare; none like them.
 </p><p>2. Rich; surpassing valuation.
 </p><p>3. Beautifying those who wear them.
 </p><p>4. Glittering with an internal and essential splendour, in the darkness of this world.
 </p><p>5. Realising in truth the old superstitions regarding precious stones having medicinal and magic virtues.
 </p><p>II. The cabinet, "My soul."
 </p><p>1. Exactly made to receive the jewels.
 </p><p>2. A wonderful piece of divine workmanship; but all ruined and marred unless applied to the use designed.
 </p><p>3. The only receptacle out of which the genuine beauty of God's testimonies can so shine as to excite the admiration of beholders.
 </p><p>III. The lack that keeps all safe, "I love them exceedingly."
 </p><p>1. Love is the strongest holdfast in the universe.
 </p><p>2. It is needed, for ten thousand thieves prowl around to steal from us the treasure.
 </p><p>3. A love "exceedingly" is a heavenly patent; no ingenuity can pick it; it is fire-proof and burglar-proof against hell itself. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#168">Psa 119:168</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The claim of God's word upon our utmost obedience. "I have kept thy precepts and thy testimonies." He does not mean that he had kept them perfectly; for that were to contradict other expressions in the Psalm. He means that he kept them sincerely and strove to keep them perfectly, as one who realized their claim upon him.
 </p><p>1. The whole word is divine, an equal authority pervades every precept; no distinction should be made of more or less obligation.
 </p><p>2. The whole word is pure and right; expediency, or making the measure and manner of obedience suitable to our own purpose, is a false principle; to be carefully distinguished from righteous expediency, which is the foregoing of a personal right in consideration of another's benefit.
 </p><p>3. The moral code of the word is a unity; obedience is like a connected chain, a wilful flaw in one link renders all useless.
 </p><p>II. The consciousness which greatly helps obedience, "For all my ways are before thee."
 </p><p>1. "Are before thee," as plainly seen by thee.
 </p><p>2. "Are before thee," constantly observed.
 </p><p>3. "Are before thee;" deliberately placed before thee by me, that they may be corrected and directed. - J. F.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#168">Psa 119:168</a>. - "All my ways are before thee."
 </p><p>I. The saint's delight.
 </p><p>II. The sinner's distress. - W. W.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#168">Psa 119:168</a> (second clause). - 
 </p><p>I. Necessarily so: for thou art the omniscient God, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa139.htm#003">Psa 139:3</a>.
 </p><p>II. Voluntarily so, for I choose to walk in thy sight. See <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa116.htm#009">Psa 116:9</a>.
 </p><p>III. Consciously and blessedly so, for the light of thy countenance inspires and gladdens me. See <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa089.htm#015">Psa 89:15</a>. - W. H. D. P.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#168">Psa 119:168</a> (second clause). - Living in the sight of God. Actually the case with all; designedly the case of the godly; happily the case of the favoured; preeminently the case of those who abide in fellowship.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#168">Psa 119:168</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The practical and doctrinal teachings of God before us.
 </p><p>II. All our ways before him.
 </p><p>III. The sort of conduct which these two causes will produce.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#169">Psa 119:169</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#170">Psa 119:170</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The singular dignity of prayer. We are on earth, but our prayers pass the seraphim and "come near before God."
 </p><p>II. The powerful right of prayer - to urge with God his own word: "according to thy word."
 </p><p>III. The triumphant possibilities of prayer. Blessing us in mind and estate. For time and eternity. "Give me understanding." "Deliver me."
 </p><p>IV. The amazing license accorded to prayer. To double and reiterate its requests (as here). - W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#169">Psa 119:169</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Admission to the royal court.
 </p><p>II. Instruction from the royal throne.
 </p><p>III. Reliance on the royal word.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#170">Psa 119:170-174</a>. - The pleader: <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#170">Psa 119:170</a>. The singer: <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#171">Psa 119:171</a>. The preacher: <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#172">Psa 119:172</a>. The worker: <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#173">Psa 119:173</a>. The waiter: <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#174">Psa 119:174</a>.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#170">Psa 119:170</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Access sought.
 </p><p>II. Answer entreated.
 </p><p>III. Argument employed.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#171">Psa 119:171</a>. - Taught; taught to praise; praising; praising for being taught.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#171">Psa 119:171</a>. - Learning to sing by learning to obey.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#171">Psa 119:171</a>. - The Happy Scholar.
 </p><p>I. He rejoices in the lesson he has learnt.
 </p><p>II. In the Teacher who has taught him.
 </p><p>III. Looks forward to the end of his lesson as the time for the full singing of his song. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#171">Psa 119:171</a>. - Lessons in Praise. - 
 </p><p>I. It is saints' work.
 </p><p>II. It is sacred work, not to be hurriedly rushed into.
 </p><p>III. It needs Spirit-instructed singers. - W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#172">Psa 119:172</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The orator: "My tongue shall speak."
 </p><p>II. His chosen theme: "of thy word."
 </p><p>III. His inward impulse: "for all thy commandments are righteousness."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#172">Psa 119:172</a>. - Savoury Speech.
 </p><p>I. A resolution all believers should make.
 </p><p>II. The qualification all believers should seek (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa045.htm#001">Psa 45:1</a>; <a href="../../../bib/kjv/mat012.htm#034">Mat 12:34</a>, <a href="../../../bib/kjv/mat012.htm#035">Mat 12:35</a>).
 </p><p>III. The edification believers would thus secure. - C. A. D.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#173">Psa 119:173</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. "To will is present with me."
 </p><p>II. "How to perform that which I would, I find not."
 </p><p>III. "Help, Lord."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#173">Psa 119:173</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Help needed to keep the divine precepts.
 </p><p>II. Help sought: "Let thy hand," etc. We should choose nothing and do nothing in which we cannot ask help from God. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#173">Psa 119:173</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. God's Hand.
 </p><p>1. Its warm hold (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/joh010.htm#029">Joh 10:29</a>).
 </p><p>2. Its wealth of contents (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa104.htm#028">Psa 104:28</a>).
 </p><p>3. Its heavy blow (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa039.htm#010">Psa 39:10</a>).
 </p><p>4. Its weight (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/sa1005.htm#011">Sa1 5:11</a>).
 </p><p>5. Its saving reach (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/isa059.htm#001">Isa 59:1</a>).
 </p><p>6. Its sweet shadow (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/isa049.htm#002">Isa 49:2</a>), etc.
 </p><p>II. The saint plucks him by the sleeve: "Let thy hand help me."
 </p><p>1. His humble representation.
 </p><p>2. His down-drawing of the hand of God. - W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#173">Psa 119:173</a>. - "Let thy hand help me."
 </p><p>I. Thy reconciling hand: "stretched out."
 </p><p>II. Thy comforting hand; like that which touched Daniel and John.
 </p><p>III. Thy supplying hand. "Thou openest thy hand," etc.
 </p><p>IV. Thy protecting hand: "all his saints are in thy hand": <a href="../../../bib/kjv/deu033.htm#003">Deu 33:3</a>. "Great Shepherd of the sheep."
 </p><p>V. Thy supporting hand: "I will uphold thee."
 </p><p>VI. Thy governing hand: "all my times are in thy hand."
 </p><p>VII. Thy chastening hand: "thy hand was heavy upon me."
 </p><p>VIII. Thy prospering hand: "the hand of the Lord was with," etc. - W. J.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#174">Psa 119:174</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Jacob's longings.
 </p><p>II. Moses' choice.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#174">Psa 119:174</a>. - God's servant drinking at salvation's well, but unsated.
 </p><p>I. Longing yielding to delight.
 </p><p>1. At God's salvation.
 </p><p>2. At the rich Scripture inventory.
 </p><p>II. Delight bringing forth further longing.
 </p><p>1. For deeper discoveries in the word.
 </p><p>2. Richer experiences in the life.
 </p><p>3. Heaven's consummation. - W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#174">Psa 119:174</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. Sighings for heaven. Holiness, happiness, God.
 </p><p>II. Sips by the way. The word of God, the will of God, service of God, the God in all. - W. B. H.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#174">Psa 119:174</a>. - "I have longed for thy salvation." Thy holy salvation. Thy full salvation. Thy free salvation. Thy present salvation. Thy permanent salvation. - W. J.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#174">Psa 119:174</a>. - "I have longed," etc. This longing arises,
 </p><p>I. From a painful consciousness of the need of salvation.
 </p><p>II. From a perception of the glories of God's salvation.
 </p><p>III. From the promises which give assurance of the possibility of obtaining this salvation.
 </p><p>IV. From the gracious promptings of the Holy Ghost. - W. H. J. P.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#175">Psa 119:175</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The highest life.
 </p><p>II. The highest occupation.
 </p><p>III. Both dependent on the highest aid.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#175">Psa 119:175</a>. - Praise.
 </p><p>I. The noblest employment of life - to praise God.
 </p><p>II. The noblest presentation of praise - the holy life.
 </p><p>III. The noblest application of divine judgments - to inspire praise.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#176">Psa 119:176</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. My confession: "I have gone astray."
 </p><p>II. My profession: "thy servant."
 </p><p>III. My petition: "seek thy servant."
 </p><p>IV. My plea: "for I do not forget." etc.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#176">Psa 119:176</a>. - 
 </p><p>I. The confession: "I have gone astray."
 </p><p>II. The petition: "Seek thy servant."
 </p><p>III. The plea, "For I do not," etc. - G. R.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#176">Psa 119:176</a>. - The last verse as such. The closing minor cadence.
 </p><p>I. The highest flights of human devotion must end in confession of sin, "I have gone astray."
 </p><p>II. The sincerest professions of human fidelity must give place to the acknowledgment of helplessness, "seek thy servant."
 </p><p>III. The loftiest human declarations of love to God's law must come down to the mournful acknowledgment that we have only not forgotten it. - C. A. D. 
 </p><p align="center"><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#001">Psalms 119:1</a></p> 
 <p><span class="margnote"><font color="GREEN" size="-1"><a name="an_001">psa 119:1</a></font></span></p>
 <p>1 Blessed are the undefiled in the way, who walk in the law of the Lord.
 </p><p>2 Blessed are they that keep his testimonies, and that seek him with the whole heart.
 </p><p>3 They also do no iniquity: they walk in his ways.
 </p><p>4 Thou hast commanded us to keep thy precepts diligently.
 </p><p>5 O that my ways were directed to keep thy statutes!
 </p><p>6 Then shall I not be ashamed, when I have respect unto all thy commandments.
 </p><p>7 I will praise thee with uprightness of heart, when I shall have learned thy righteous judgments.
 </p><p>8 I will keep thy statutes: O forsake me not utterly.
 </p><p>These first eight verses are taken up with a contemplation of the blessedness which comes through keeping the statutes of the Lord. The subject is treated in a devout manner rather than in a didactic style. Heart-fellowship with God is enjoyed through a love of that word which is God's way of communing with the soul by his Holy Spirit. Prayer and praise and all sorts of devotional acts and feelings gleam through the verses like beams of sunlight through an olive grove. You are not only instructed, but influenced to holy emotion, and helped to express the same.
 </p><p>Lovers of God's holy words are blessed, because they are preserved from defilement (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#001">Psa 119:1</a>), because they are made practically holy (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#002">Psa 119:2</a> and <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#003">Psa 119:3</a>), and are led to follow after God sincerely and intensely (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#002">Psa 119:2</a>). It is seen that this holy walking must be desirable because God commands it (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#004">Psa 119:4</a>); therefore the pious soul prays for it (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#005">Psa 119:5</a>), and feels that its comfort and courage must depend upon obtaining it (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#006">Psa 119:6</a>). In the prospect of answered prayer, yea, while the prayer is being answered the heart is full of thankfulness (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#007">Psa 119:7</a>), and is fixed in solemn resolve not to miss the blessing if the Lord will give enabling grace (<a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#008">Psa 119:8</a>).
 </p><p>The changes are rung upon the words "way" - "undefiled in the way," "walk in his ways," "O that my ways were directed"; "keep" - "keep his testimonies," "keep thy precepts diligently," "directed to keep," "I will keep"; and "walk" - "walk in the law," "walk in his ways." Yet there is no tautology, nor is the same thought repeated, though to the careless reader it may seem so.
 </p><p>The change from statements about others and about the Lord to more personal dealing with God begins in <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#003">Psa 119:3</a>, and becomes more clear as we advance, till in the later verses the communion becomes most intense and soul moving. O that every reader may feel the glow.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#001">Psa 119:1</a>
 </p><p>"Blessed." The Psalmist is so enraptured with the word of God that he regards it as his highest ideal of blessedness to be conformed to it. He has gazed on the beauties of the perfect law, and, as if this verse were the sum and outcome of all his emotions, he exclaims, "Blessed is the man whose life is the practical transcript of the will of God." True religion is not cold and dry; it has its exclamations and raptures. We not only judge the keeping of God's law to be a wise and proper thing, but we are warmly enamoured of its holiness, and cry out in adoring wonder, "Blessed are the undefiled!" meaning thereby, that we eagerly desire to become such ourselves, and wish for no greater happiness than to be perfectly holy. It may be that the writer laboured under a sense of his own faultiness, and therefore envied the blessedness of those whose walk had been more pure and clean; indeed, the very contemplation of the perfect law of the Lord upon which he now entered was quite enough to make him bemoan his own imperfections, and sigh for the blessedness of an undefiled walk.
 </p><p>True religion is always practical, for it does not permit us to delight ourselves in a perfect rule without exciting in us a longing to be conformed to it in our daily lives. A blessing belongs to those who hear and read and understand the word of the Lord; yet is it a far greater blessing to be actually obedient to it, and to carry out in our walk and conversation what we learn in our searching of the Scriptures. Purity in our way and walk is the truest blessedness.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#001">Psa 119:1</a> is not only a preface to the whole Psalm, but it may also be regarded as the text upon which the rest is a discourse. It is similar to the benediction of the first Psalm, which is set in the forefront of the entire book: there is a likeness between this <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#001">Psa 119:1</a> and the Psalter, and this is one point of it, that it begins with a benediction. In this, too, we see some foreshadowing of the Son of David, who began his great sermon as David began his great Psalm. It is well to open our mouth with blessings. When we cannot bestow them, we can show the way of obtaining them, and even if we do not yet possess them ourselves, it may be profitable to contemplate them, that our desires may be excited, and our souls moved to seek after them. Lord, if I am not yet so blessed to be among the undefiled in thy way, yet I will think much of the happiness which these enjoy, and set it before me as my life's ambition.
 </p><p>As David thus begins his Psalm, so should young men begin their lives, so should new converts commence their profession, so should all Christians begin every day. Settle it in your hearts as a first postulate and sure rule of practical science that holiness is happiness, and that it is our wisdom first to seek the Kingdom of God and his righteousness. Well begun is half done. To start with a true idea of blessedness is beyond measure important. Man began with being blessed in his innocence, and if our fallen race is ever to be blessed again, it must find it where it lost it at the beginning, namely, in conformity to the command of the Lord.
 </p><p>"The undefiled in the way." They are in the way, the right way, the way of the Lord, and they keep that way, walking with holy carefulness and washing their feet daily, lest they be found spotted by the flesh. They enjoy great blessedness in their own souls; indeed, they have a foretaste of heaven where the blessedness lieth much in being absolutely undefiled; and could they continue utterly and altogether without defilement, doubtless they would have the days of heaven upon the earth. Outward evil would little hurt us if we were entirely rid of the evil of sin, an attainment which with the best of us lies still in the region of desire, and is not yet fully reached, though we have so clear a view of it that we see it to be blessedness itself; and therefore we eagerly press towards it.
 </p><p>He whose life is in a gospel sense undefiled, is blessed, because he could never have reached this point if a thousand blessings had not already been bestowed on him. By nature we are defiled and out of the way, and we must therefore have been washed in the atoning blood to remove defilement, and we must have been converted by the power of the Holy Ghost, or we should not have been turned into the way of peace, nor be undefiled in it. Nor is this all, for the continual power of grace is needed to keep a believer in the right way, and to preserve him from pollution. All the blessings of the covenant must have been in a measure poured upon those who from day to day have been enabled to perfect holiness In the fear of the Lord. Their way is the evidence of their being the blessed of the Lord.
 </p><p>David speaks of a high degree of blessedness; for some are in the way, and are true servants of God, but they are as yet faulty in many ways and bring defilement upon themselves. Others who walk in the light more fully, and maintain closer communion with God, are enabled to keep themselves unspotted from the world, and these enjoy far more peace and joy than their less watchful brethren. Doubtless, the more complete our sanctification the more intense our blessedness. Christ is our way, and we are not only alive in Christ, but we are to live in Christ; the sorrow is that we bespatter his holy way with our selfishness, self-exaltation, wilfulness, and carnality, and so we miss a great measure of the blessedness which is in him as our way. A believer who errs is still saved, but the joy of his salvation is not experienced by him; he is rescued but not enriched, greatly borne with, but not greatly blessed.
 </p><p>How easily may defilement come upon us even in our holy things, yea, even in the way. We may even come from public or private worship with defilement upon the conscience gathered when we were on our knees. There was no floor to the tabernacle but the desert sand, and hence the priests at the altar were under frequent necessity to wash their feet, and by the kind foresight of their God, the laver stood ready for their cleansing, even as for us our Lord Jesus still stands ready to wash our feet, that we may be clean every whit. Thus our text sets forth the blessedness of the apostles in the upper room when Jesus had said of them, "Ye are clean."
 </p><p>What blessedness awaits those who follow the Lamb whithersoever he goeth, and are preserved from the evil which is in the world through lust. These shall be the envy of all mankind "in that day." Though now they despise them as precise fanatics and Puritans, the most prosperous of sinners shall then wish that they could change places with them. O my soul, seek thou thy blessedness in following hard after thy Lord, who was holy, harmless, undefiled; for there hast thou found peace hitherto, and there wilt thou find it for ever.
 </p><p>"Who walk in the law of the Lord." In them is found habitual holiness. Their walk, their common everyday life is obedience unto the Lord. They live by rule, that rule the command of the Lord God. Whether they eat or drink, or whatsoever they do, they do all in the name of their great Master and Exemplar. To them religion is nothing out of the way, it is their everyday walk: it moulds their common actions as well as their special devotions. This ensures blessedness. He who walks in God's law walks in God's company, and he must be blessed; he has God's smile, God's strength, God's secret with him, and how can he be otherwise than blessed?
 </p><p>The holy life is a walk, a steady progress, a quiet advance, a lasting continuance. Enoch walked with God. Good men always long to be better, and hence they go forward. Good men are never idle, and hence they do not lie down or loiter, but they are still walking onward to their desired end. They are not hurried, and worried, and flurried, and so they keep the even tenor of their way, walking steadily towards heaven; and they are not in perplexity as to how to conduct themselves, for they have a perfect rule, which they are happy to walk by. The law of the Lord is not irksome to them; its commandments are not grievous, and its restrictions are not slavish in their esteem. It does not appear to them to be an impossible law, theoretically admirable but practically absurd, but they walk by it and in it. They do not consult it now and then as a sort of rectifier of their wanderings, but they use it as a chart for their daily sailing, a map of the road for their life-journey. Nor do they ever regret that they have entered upon the path of obedience, else they would leave it, and that without difficulty, for a thousand temptations offer them opportunity to return; their continued walk in the law of the Lord is their best testimony to the blessedness of such a condition of life. Yes, they are blessed even now. The Psalmist himself bore witness to the fact: he had tried and proved it, and wrote it down, as a fact which defied all denial. Here it stands in the forefront of David's magnum opus, written on the topmost line of his greatest Psalm - "Blessed are they who walk in the law of the Lord." Rough may be the way, stern the rule, hard the discipline, - all these we know and more, - but a thousand heaped-up blessednesses are still found in godly living, for which we bless the Lord.
 </p><p>We have in this verse blessed persons who enjoy five blessed things, A blessed way, blessed purity, a blessed law, given by a blessed Lord, and a blessed walk therein; to which we may add the blessed testimony of the Holy Ghost given in this very passage that they are in very deed the blessed of the Lord.
 </p><p>The blessedness which is thus set before us we must aim at, but we must not think to obtain it without earnest effort. David has a great deal to say about it; his discourse in this Psalm is long and solemn, and it is a hint to us that the way of perfect obedience is not learned in a day; there must be precept upon precept, line upon line, and after efforts long enough to be compared with the 176 verses of this Psalm we may still have to cry, "I have gone astray like a lost sheep; seek thy servant; for I do not forget thy commandments."
 </p><p>It must, however, be our plan to keep the word of the Lord much upon our minds; for this discourse upon blessedness has for its pole-star the testimony of the Lord, and only by daily communion with the Lord by his word can we hope to learn his way, to be purged from defilement, and to be made to walk in his statutes. We set out upon this exposition with blessedness before us; we see the way to it, and we know where the law of it is to be found: let us pray that as we pursue our meditation we may grow into the habit and walk of obedience, and so feel the blessedness of which we read.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#002">Psa 119:2</a>
 </p><p>"Blessed are they that keep his testimonies." What! A second blessing? Yes, they are doubly blessed whose outward life is supported by an inward zeal for God's glory. In <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#001">Psa 119:1</a> we had an undefiled way, and it was taken for granted that the purity in the way was not mere surface work, but was attended by the inward truth and life which comes of divine grace. Here that which was implied is expressed. Blessedness is ascribed to those who treasure up the testimonies of the Lord: in which is implied that they search the Scriptures, that they come to an understanding of them, that they love them, and then that they continue in the practice of them. We must first get a thing before we can keep it. In order to keep it well we must get a firm grip of it: we cannot keep in the heart that which we have not heartily embraced by the affections. God's word is his witness or testimony to grand and important truths which concern himself and our relation to him: this we should desire to know; knowing it, we should believe it; believing it, we should love it; and loving it, we should hold it fast against all comers. There is a doctrinal keeping of the word when we are ready to die for its defence, and a practical keeping of it when we actually live under its power. Revealed truth is precious as diamonds, and should be kept or treasured up in the memory and in the heart as jewels In a casket, or as the law was kept in the ark; this however is not enough, for it is meant for practical use, and therefore it must be kept or followed, as men keep to a path, or to a line of business. If we keep God's testimonies they will keep us; they will keep us right in opinion, comfortable in spirit, holy in conversation, and hopeful in expectation. If they were ever worth having - and no thoughtful person will question that - then they are worth keeping; their designed effect does not come through a temporary seizure of them, but by a persevering keeping of them: "in keeping of them there is great reward."
 </p><p>We are bound to keep with all care the word of God, because it is his testimonies. He gave them to us, but they are still his own. We are to keep them as a watchman guards his master's house, as a steward husbands his lord's goods, as a shepherd keeps his employer's flock. We shall have to give an account, for we are put in trust with the gospel, and woe to us if we be found unfaithful. We cannot fight a good fight, nor finish our course, unless we keep the faith. To this end the Lord must keep us: only those who are kept by the power of God unto salvation will ever be able to keep his testimonies. What a blessedness is therefore evidenced and testified by a careful belief in God's word, and a continual obedience thereunto. God has blessed them, in blessing them, and will bless them for ever. That blessedness which David saw in others he realized for himself, for in <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#168">Psa 119:168</a> he says, "I have kept thy precepts and thy testimonies," and in <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#054">Psa 119:54-56</a> he traces his joyful songs and happy memories to this same keeping of the law, and he confesses, "This I had because I kept thy precepts." Doctrines which we teach to others we should experience for ourselves.
 </p><p>"And that seek him with the whole heart." Those who keep the Lord's testimonies are sure to seek after himself. If his word is precious we may be sure that he himself is still more so. Personal dealing with a personal God is the longing of all those who have allowed the word of the Lord to have its full effect upon them. If we once really know the power of the gospel we must seek the God of the gospel. "O that I knew where I might find HIM," will be our whole-hearted cry. See the growth which these sentences indicate: first, in the way, then walking in it, then finding and keeping the treasure of truth, and to crown all, seeking after the Lord of the way himself. Note also that the further a soul advances in grace the more spiritual and divine are its longings; an outward walk does not content the gracious soul, nor even the treasured testimonies; it reaches out in due time after God himself, and when it in a measure finds him, still yearns for more of him, and seeks him still.
 </p><p>Seeking after God signifies a desire to commune with him more closely, to follow him more fully, to enter into more perfect union with his mind and will, to promote his glory, and to realize completely all that he is to holy hearts. The blessed man has God already, and for this reason he seeks him. This may seem a contradiction: it is only a paradox.
 </p><p>God is not truly sought by the cold researches of the brain: we must seek him with the heart. Love reveals itself to love: God manifests his heart to the heart of his people. It is in vain that we endeavour to comprehend him by reason; we must apprehend him by affection. But the heart must not be divided with many objects if the Lord is to be sought by us. God is one, and we shall not know him till our heart is one. A broken heart need not be distressed at this, for no heart is so whole in its seekings after God as a heart which is broken, whereof every fragment sighs and cries after the great Father's face. It is the divided heart which the doctrine of the text censures, and strange to say, in scriptural phraseology, a heart may be divided and not broken, and it may be broken but not divided; and yet again it may be broken and be whole, and it never can be whole until it is broken. When our whole heart seeks the holy God in Christ Jesus it has come to him of whom it is written, "as many as touched him were made perfectly whole."
 </p><p>That which the Psalmist admires in this verse he claims in <a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#010">Psa 119:10</a>, where he says, "With my whole heart have I sought thee." It is well when admiration of a virtue leads to the attainment of it. Those who do not believe in the blessedness of seeking the Lord will not be likely to arouse their hearts to the pursuit, but he who calls another blessed because of the grace which he sees in him is on the way to gaining the same grace for himself.
 </p><p>If those who seek the Lord are blessed, what shall be said of those who actually dwell with him and know that he is theirs?
 </p><p>"To those who fall, how kind thou art!
 </p><p>How good to those who seek
 </p><p>But what to those who find? Ah! this
 </p><p>Nor tongue nor pen can show!
 </p><p>The love of Jesus - what it is,
 </p><p>None but his loved ones know."
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#003">Psa 119:3</a>
 </p><p>"They also do no iniquity." Blessed indeed would those men be of whom this could be asserted without reserve and without explanation: we shall have reached the region of pure blessedness when we altogether cease from sin. Those who follow the word of God do no iniquity, the rule is perfect, and if it be constantly followed no fault will arise. Life, to the outward observer, at any rate, lies much in doing, and he who in his doings never swerves from equity, both towards God and man, has hit upon the way of perfection, and we may be sure that his heart is right. See how a whole heart leads to the avoidance of evil, for the Psalmist says, "That seek him with the whole heart. They also do no iniquity." We fear that no man can claim to be absolutely without sin, and yet we trust there are many who do not designedly, wilfully, knowingly, and continuously do anything that is wicked, ungodly, or unjust. Grace keeps the life righteous as to act even when the Christian has to bemoan the transgressions of the heart. Judged as men should be judged by their fellows, according to such just rules as men make for men, the true people of God do no iniquity: they are honest, upright, and chaste, and touching justice and morality they are blameless. Therefore are they happy.
 </p><p>"They walk in his ways." They attend not only to the great main highway of the law, but to the smaller paths of the particular precepts. As they will perpetrate no sin of commission, so do they labour to be free from every sin of omission. It is not enough to them to be blameless, they wish also to be actively righteous. A hermit may escape into solitude that he may do no iniquity, but a saint lives in society that he may serve his God by walking in his ways. We must be positively as well as negatively right: we shall not long keep the second unless we attend to the first, for men will be walking one way or another, and if they do not follow the path of God's law they will soon do iniquity. The surest way to abstain from evil is to be fully occupied in doing good. This verse describes believers as they exist among us: although they have their faults and infirmities, yet they hate evil, and will not permit themselves to do it; they love the ways of truth, right and true godliness, mid habitually they walk therein. They do not claim to be absolutely perfect except in their desires, and there they are pure indeed, for they pant to be kept from all sin, and to be led into all holiness.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#004">Psa 119:4</a>
 </p><p>"Thou hast commanded us to keep thy precepts diligently." So that when we have done all we are unprofitable servants, we have done only that which it,was our duty to have done, seeing we have our Lord's command for it. God's precepts require careful obedience: there is no keeping them by accident. Some give to God a careless service, a sort of hit or miss obedience, but the Lord has not commanded such service, nor will he accept it. His law demands the love of all our heart, soul, mind, and strength; and a careless religion has none of these. We are also called to zealous obedience. We are to keep the precepts abundantly: the vessels of obedience should be filled to the brim, and the command carried out to the full of its meaning. As a man diligent in business arouses himself to do as much trade as he can, so must we be eager to serve the Lord as much as possible. Nor must we spare pains to do so, for a diligent obedience will also be laborious and self-denying. Those who are diligent in business rise up early and sit up late, and deny themselves much of comfort and repose. They are not soon tired, or if they are they persevere even with aching brow and weary eyes. So should we serve the Lord. Such a Master deserves diligent servants; such service he demands, and will be content with nothing less. How seldom do men render it, and hence many through their negligence miss the double blessing spoken of in this Psalm.
 </p><p>Some are diligent in superstition and will worship; be it ours to be diligent in keeping God's precepts. It is no use travelling fast if we are not in the right road. Men have been diligent in a losing business, and the more they have traded the more they have lost: this is bad enough in commerce, we cannot afford to have it so in our religion.
 </p><p>God has not commanded us to be diligent in making precepts, but in keeping them. Some bind yokes upon their own necks, and make bonds and rules for others: but the wise course is to be satisfied with the rules of holy Scripture, and to strive to keep them all, in all places, towards all men, and in all respects. If we do not this, we may become eminent in our own religion, but we shall not have kept the command of God, nor shall we be accepted of him.
 </p><p>The Psalmist began with the third person: he is now coming near home, and has already reached the first person plural, according to our version; we shall soon hear him crying out personally and for himself. As the heart glows with love to holiness, we long to have a personal interest in it. The word of God is a heart-affecting book, and when we begin to sing its praises it soon comes home to us, and sets us praying to be ourselves conformed to its teachings.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#005">Psa 119:5</a>
 </p><p>"O that my ways were directed to keep thy statutes!" Divine commands should direct us in the subject of our prayers. We cannot of ourselves keep God's statutes as he would have them kept, and yet we long to do so: what resort have we but prayer? We must ask the Lord to work our works in us, or we shall never work out his commandments. This verse is a sigh of regret because the Psalmist feels that he has not kept the precepts diligently, it is a cry of weakness appealing for help to one who can aid, it is a request of bewilderment from one who has lost his way and would fain be directed in it, and it is a petition of faith from one who loves God and trusts in him for grace.
 </p><p>Our ways are by nature opposed to the way of God, and must be turned by the Lord's direction in another direction from that which they originally take or they will lead us down to destruction. God can direct the mind and will without violating our free agency, and he will do so in answer to prayer; in fact, he has begun the work already in those who are heartily praying after the fashion of this verse. It is for present holiness that the desire arises in the heart. O that it were so now with me: but future persevering holiness is also meant, for he longs for grace to keep henceforth and for ever the statutes of the Lord.
 </p><p>The sigh of the text is really a prayer, though it does not exactly take that form. Desires and longings are of the essence of supplication, and it little matters what shape they take. "O that" is as acceptable a prayer as "Our Father."
 </p><p>One would hardly have expected a prayer for direction; rather should we have looked for a petition for enabling. Can we not direct ourselves? What if we cannot row, we can steer. The Psalmist herein confesses that even for the smallest part of his duty he felt unable without grace. He longed for the Lord to influence his will, as well as to strengthen his hands. We want a rod to point out the way as much as a staff to support us in it.
 </p><p>The longing of the text is prompted by admiration of the blessedness of holiness, by a contemplation of the righteous man's beauty of character, and by a reverent awe of the command of God. It is a personal application to the writer's own case of the truths which he had been considering. "O that my ways," etc. It were well if all who hear and read the word would copy this example and turn all that they hear into prayer. We should have more keepers of the statutes if we had more who sighed and cried after the grace to do so.
 </p><p><a href="../../../bib/kjv/psa119.htm#006">Psa 119:6</a>
 </p><p>"Then shall I not be ashamed." He had known shame, and here he rejoices in the prospect of being freed from it. Sin brings shame, and when sin is gone, the reason for being ashamed is banished. What a deliverance this is, for to some men death is preferable to shame! "When I have respect unto all thy commandments." When he respects God he shall respect himself and be respected. Whenever we err we prepare ourselves for confusion of face and sinking of heart: if no one else is ashamed of me I shall be ashamed of myself if I do iniquity. Our first parents never knew shame till they made the acquaintance of the old serpent, and it never left them till their gracious God had covered them with sacrificial skins. Disobedience made them naked and ashamed. We, ourselves, will always have cause for shame till every sin is vanquished, and every duty is observed. When we pay a continual and universal respect to the will of the Lord, then we shall be able to look ourselves in the face in the looking-glass of the law, and we shall not blush at the sight of men or devils, however eager their malice may be to lay somewhat to our charge.
 </p><p>Many suffer from excessive diffidence. and this verse suggests a cure. An abiding sense of duty will make us bold, we shall be afraid to be afraid. No shame in the presence of man will hinder us when the fear of God has taken full possession of our minds. When we are on the king's highway by daylight, and are engaged upon royal business, we need ask no man's leave. It would be a dishonour to a king to be ashamed of his livery and his service; no such shame should ever crimson the cheek of a Christian, nor will it if he has due reverence for the Lord his God. There is nothing to be ashamed of in a holy life; a man may be ashamed of his pride, ashamed of his wealth, ashamed of his own children, but he will never be ashamed of having in all things regarded the will of the Lord his God.
 </p><p>It is worthy of remark that David promises himself no immunity from shame till he has carefully paid homage to all the precepts.
